FAT E O F D R U G B I O M A R K E R S I N L A B O R ATO RY- S C A L E
S E WA G E B I O R E A C TO R S : E X P E R I M E N TA L A N D M O D E L L I N G
A S S E S S M E N T
andreas libonati brock
==PAGE:2==
Andreas Libonati Brock: Fate of drug biomarkers in laboratory-scale sewage biore-
actors: experimental and modelling assessment, Master’s thesis c⃝September
2015
ENV-MSc-2015-s082841
supervisors:
Associate Professor Benedek Gy. Plósz, PhD, DTU Environment
PhD student Pedram Ramin, DTU Environment
PhD student Fabio Polesel, DTU Environment
==PAGE:3==
A B S T R A C T
The objective of this thesis was to assess the fate of different illicit drugs
and their major human metabolites in laboratory-scale sewage bioreac-
tors. In total 24 different compounds were analysed: Cocaine (COC),
benzoylecgonine (BE), ecgonine methyl ester (EME), cocaethylene; heroin
(HER), 6-acetylmorphine (6-MAM), morphine (MOR), morphine-3-β-D-
glucuronide (M3G), codeine (COD), norcodeine (NORCOD); ketamine
(KET),
norketamine
(NORKET);
3,4-methylenedioxymethamphetamine
(MDMA), 3,4-methylene-dioxyamphetamine (MDA); mephedrone (MEPH);
methadone
(MTD),
2-ethylidene-1,5-dimethyl-3,3-diphenylpyrrolidine
(EDDP);
methamphetamine
(METH),
amphetamine
(AMPH);
∆9-
tetrahydrocannabinol
(THC),
11-nor-9-carboxy-∆9-THC
(THC-COOH),
11-hydroxy-∆9-THC (THC-OH), cannabidiol (CBD), and cannabinol (CBN).
Two annular rotating bioﬁlm reactors (ARBR) were used to assess the con-
tribution of sewer bioﬁlm, and two generic continuously stirred batch reac-
tors were used to assess the contribution of the suspend biomass present in
raw wastewater. The transformation and sorption under both aerobic and
anaerobic conditions were assessed. The results showed that the transfor-
mation of COC, BE, EME, and CE is to a large extent solely abiotic. HER
was found to be transformed abiotically and biotically. The transformation
was augmented by the presence of both suspended biomass and the bioﬁlm.
6-MAM and M3G were found to be transformed to MOR by the bioﬁlm
and suspended biomass. Moreover, MOR was found to be transformed by
the suspended biomass. Under anaerobic conditions, NORCOD and MDMA
were transformed by the bioﬁlm. MEPH was found to be transformed both
abiotically and biotically. Under aerobic conditions, the biotic transforma-
tion rate was found to be considerably higher than under anaerobic con-
ditions. MTD and EDDP were transformed abiotically and biotically. Un-
der aerobic conditions the transformation rate was considerably higher, and
the presence of bioﬁlm considerably augmented the transformation rate of
the compounds. The results suggest that MTD is transformed more to an-
other metabolite than EDDP. AMPH was found to be transformed by the
aerobic bioﬁlm. NORKET, KET, COD, MDA, and METH were found to be
stable under all experimental conditions. All targeted biomarkers except
codeine and morphine were found to sorb to either the suspended solids/
biomass or the bioﬁlm under at least one of the redox conditions. The Acti-
vated Sludge Modelling framework for trace Xenobiotic chemicals (ASM-X)
was used to estimate the liquid-solid partitioning coefﬁcients and the bi-
otic and abiotic transformation rates for the suspended biomass and ARBR
system. To describe the primary metabolism of the suspended aerobic het-
erotrophic biomass, the Wastewater Aerobic/anaerobic Transformations in
Sewers (WATS) model was used. An experimental method using manomet-
ric respirometry was developed and used to estimate the kinetic parameters.
iii

I gratefully acknowledge the contribution of my supervisors Benedek Gy.
Plósz, Pedram Ramin, and Fabio Polesel. I would especially like to thank
Fabio and Pedram for their indispensable suggestions and help throughout
the ordeal. Furthermore, I especially want to thank Pedram for the chemical
analysis performed at KWR in the Netherlands and the subsequent data
retrieval from the chromatograms as well as for our collaboration in the
laboratory.
Moreover, I would also like to acknowledge Dorottya S. Wagner and Borja
V. Perez for their help and presence in the laboratory as well as for the
procurement of the wastewater used to feed the bioreactors.
Furthermore, I extend my sincerest gratitude to ﬁeld technician Flemming
Møller, laboratory technician Hector H. C. Garcia, head of laboratory section
Mona Refstrup, head of laboratory section Sinh H. Nguyen, head of labora-
tory section Jens S. Sørensen, and head of laboratory Hanne B. Rasmussen
for assisting and helping me with all the practicalities in the laboratory and
procurement of laboratory articles and disposables.
I would like to deeply thank my ofﬁce mates Lea Olesen, Marie Kampmann,
Anne Soﬁe Kiil, Saverio Conti, Jens Toke, Cecilie Thrysøe, Michael Steidl,
Sanita Dhaubanjar, and Christian Kjær for moral support and encourage-
ment. I especially want to thank Elena Penkarski Rodon for her help with
the more than 130 solid-phase extractions and the ﬁltration of centrifuged
raw wastewater for the bioﬁlm experiments.
Furthermore, I would like to thank the laboratory technicians at Mølleåvær-
ket, Dorte Hovland and Susanne Rasmussen for assisting us with procure-
ment of wastewater and sludge. Additionally, I would like to thank the lab-
oratory technicians and plant operators at Lynetten Treatment Plant (BIO-
FOS) for allowing us to take samples for the SCORE and COST sampling
campaigns carried out in the ﬁrst weeks of March.
Lastly, I would like to thank my extraordinary girlfriend, Sophie, without
her support I surely would have succumbed and withered away.

P R E FA C E
This thesis has been completed at the Department of Environmental Engi-
neering at the Technical University. The thesis serves as a partial (albeit ﬁnal)
fulﬁlment of the MSc in Environmental Engineering with specialisation in
Environmental Chemistry and Microbiology. The thesis work amounts to
32.5 ECTS.
Andreas Libonati Brock
Kgs. Lyngby, September 2015

1
I N T R O D U C T I O N
The waste we excrete carries informations of our lives and our livestyle.
When we consume drugs these are metabolised and excreted. The analysis
of wastewater can help us understand how we live in the same way as
paleofaeces can help archaeologists discern the activities and diets of our
ancestors (Feder, 2008; Henze et al., 2008). Wastewater analysis is derived
from the fact that traces of what we personally consume will eventually be
excreted to the sewer system through our urine, faeces, sweat etc. Sewage
epidemiology uses the presence of urinary drug biomarkers in wastewater
as a tool to estimate the illicit drug use in a community.
The conventional methods used to estimate community-wide drug con-
sumption have mainly relied on hospital admissions, conﬁscations and
seizures, crime statistics, self-administered questionnaires, and national sur-
veys (Daughton, 2001; Nuijs et al., 2011a; Castiglioni et al., 2014). Surveys
are expensive to conduct and time consuming to collate and analyse, hence
a substantial lag period between the time of collection and publication ex-
ists (Castiglioni et al., 2014). Surveys are also prone to self-report bias and
sample bias (Burgard et al., 2014).
The idea to analyse the wastewater for its contents of illicit drugs was ﬁrst
proposed by Daughton (2001). To monitor the occurrence of excreted and
released illicit drugs and their metabolites in a sewer catchment, he pro-
posed to sample at the inlet of the wastewater treatment plant (WWTP).
The determined mass load of a speciﬁc compound could then be used to
back-calculate the consumption and hence estimate the consumption rates
in the catchment serviced by the WWTP. The use of wastewater analysis was
proposed as a non-intrusive tool to both assess the prevalence of illicit drug
use and to assess their potential as environmental pollutants (Daughton,
2001).
Since 2001, wastewater analysis, has seen a huge increase in publications,
attention, application, and funding (Prichard et al., 2014; Castiglioni et al.,
2014; Kasprzyk-Hordern et al., 2014; SEWPROF ITN, 2015). Jones-Lepp et al.
(2004) were the ﬁrst to report the presence of illicit drugs in the efﬂuent of
two wastewater treatment plants, and Zuccato et al. (2005) were the ﬁrst to
use the sewage epidemiology approach to estimate the cocaine consumption
in Italy by measurements of water samples collected from the River Po and
WWTPs. Since then, the approach has become widespread and many other
investigations have been made – e.g. wastewater analysis for illicit drug
residues and metabolites have, among other countries, been conducted in
Australia, Belgium, Canada, China, Croatia, Denmark, France, Italy, Norway,
Spain, United Kingdom, and the United States of America (Zuccato et al.,
2005; Boleda et al., 2007; Banta-Green et al., 2009; Terzic et al., 2010; MacLeod
and Wong, 2010; Karolak et al., 2010; Kasprzyk-Hordern et al., 2010; Nuijs
1
==PAGE:22==
2
introduction
et al., 2011b; Archer et al., 2013; Lai et al., 2013; Dyken et al., 2014a; Li et al.,
2014; Reid et al., 2014a; Subedi and Kannan, 2014).
Sewage epidemiology seeks to improve the temporal and spatial resolution
when monitoring the consumption of illicit drugs (Zuccato et al., 2008; Cas-
tiglioni et al., 2014). Wastewater analysis makes it possible to infer and detect
temporal patterns in drug use, e.g. the use of illicit drugs has been shown
to increase during the weekend and during festivities (Terzic et al., 2010;
Nuijs et al., 2011b; Plósz et al., 2013b; Bijlsma et al., 2014). Moreover, the
prospects of being able to detect new psychoactive substances (NPS) also
shows the versatility of sewage epidemiology (EMCDDA, 2011; Archer et
al., 2013; Reid et al., 2014a).
The novel approach with wastewater analysis and the traditional epidemio-
logical studies should be considered complimentary, as each approach has
its strengths and weaknesses (Castiglioni et al., 2014). Furthermore, the ap-
plicability and versatility of wastewater analysis and sewage epidemiology
also incur ethical issues which are not trivial (Hall et al., 2012), and the for-
mulation of ethical research guidelines has also received attention (Prichard
et al., 2014).
For sewage epidemiology to be extensively used in conjunction with the
existing methods and to be reliable, much effort has been devoted to the
development of analytical methods and reduction of uncertainties related
to sampling, storage, analysis, choice of biomarker, and back-calculation to
estimate the consumption (Lai et al., 2011; Baker and Kasprzyk-Hordern,
2011; Nuijs et al., 2011a; Khan and Nicell, 2011; Ort et al., 2014). Aside from
stability studies, in-sewer transformation has not been extensively explored,
however some studies have in recent years been undertaken e.g. Nuijs et al.
(2012), Plósz et al. (2013b), Bisceglia and Lippa (2014), and Senta et al. (2014).
In-sewer transformation is important as the sewage system is not only a
means of conveyance, it is also a bioreactor in which many different pro-
cesses take place (Hvitved-Jacobsen et al., 2013). Plósz et al. (2013a) iden-
tiﬁed the in-sewer processes as an area in need of more research in order
to assess the uncertainties associated when using the concentration at the
inlet to WWTPs. Do the compounds undergo signiﬁcant transformation en-
route to the treatment plant? Plósz et al. (2013b) showed that in-sewer pro-
cesses had a signiﬁcant impact when back-calculating the consumption in
a given sewer catchment. However, Castiglioni et al. (2013) estimated the
uncertainty related to in-sewer biotransformation to be less than 10%. Still,
the present knowledge of in-sewer transformation of biomarkers represents
a knowledge gap as transformation can occur in the bulk liquid phase, the
bioﬁlm, and the sediment (Lai et al., 2011; Baker and Kasprzyk-Hordern,
2011; Castiglioni et al., 2014; Bisceglia and Lippa, 2014).
Thai et al. (2014) investigated the biotransformation of ﬁve illicit drug
biomarkers in laboratory-scale bioreactors. The reactors mimicked the con-
ditions found in rising mains (anaerobic), gravity sewers (aerobic), and sus-
pended wastewater (absence of bioﬁlm). They concluded that the presence
of a bioﬁlm for some of the biomarkers resulted in a faster biotransforma-
tion and possibly also a higher variety of transformation products (Thai et
al., 2014).
==PAGE:23==
1.1 objective
3
This thesis seeks to further close the existing knowledge gap on sorption and
biotransformation of drug biomarkers under different sewer conditions.
1.1
objective
The physicochemical processes and the transformation of biomarkers are
assumed to mainly be catalysed by the suspended biomass present in the
bulk liquid phase and the attached biomass in the bioﬁlm on the pipe sur-
face. The objective of this thesis is:
• To elucidate the transformation due to the suspended biomass by con-
ducting batch experiments in generic continuously stirred reactors.
• To assess the transformation ascribed to the attached bioﬁlm by con-
ducting experiments in annular rotating bioﬁlm reactors.
• To estimate the transformation due to abiotic processes.
• Estimate the solid-liquid partitioning coefﬁcient between (a) the aque-
ous phase and the suspended solids, and (b) the aqueous phase and
the bioﬁlm.
• The effect of the redox conditions is also assessed as experiments are
conducted under aerobic conditions and under anaerobic conditions.
• A model describing the transformation and sorption processes in the
annular rotating bioﬁlm reactors and suspended biomass is developed
based on the Activated Sludge Modelling framework for trace Xenobi-
otic chemicals (ASM-X) (Plósz et al., 2012; Plósz et al., 2013b).
• The primary metabolic process of the suspended aerobic heterotrophic
biomass is simulated using the Wastewater Aerobic/anaerobic Trans-
formations in Sewers (WATS) model (Hvitved-Jacobsen et al., 2013).
==PAGE:24==
==PAGE:25==
2
L I T E R AT U R E R E V I E W
In the following chapter, the ethical aspects of wastewater analysis and
sewage epidemiology will be presented and discussed. Following this, the
physicochemical, pharmacological, and pharmacokinetic relevant informa-
tions such as metabolic pathways (in humans), and degradation pathways
in the environment are presented. This is followed by a presentation of meth-
ods and results from other comparable studies. A brief literature review on
in-sewer processes and their modelling is given.Lastly a brief presentation
of the sewer catchment and the treatment plant is provided.
2.1
ethical aspects of wastewater analysis and sewage epi-
demiology
As has been established in the introduction, Daughton (2001) proposed the
sampling of WWTP as a non-intrusive tool for analysis and estimation of
community-wide illicit drug use. The current practice in drug epidemiology
is questionnaires, surveys, drug seizures, hospitalisations etc. (Daughton,
2001; Nuijs et al., 2011a; Castiglioni et al., 2014; EMCDDA, 2015a). However,
the advancement of sampling techniques and methods such as use of a pas-
sive sampling device has increased concerns regarding how far upstream
one can take the analysis (Harman et al., 2011b; Hall et al., 2012). The ma-
jor ethical concern is with regard to the privacy of the individual and the
purposes of the scientiﬁc studies.
To better identify the ethical aspects, Hall et al. (2012) applied the ethi-
cal principles used in biomedical and epidemiological research. The ethics
and principles normally associated with biomedical and epidemiological re-
search are:
respect for autonomy
This principle entails that the privacy and con-
ﬁdentiality of the research participants are respected, and that all risks
associated with the study are conveyed to the participants. In biomedical
research this usually necessitates the informed consent of the participants
(Beauchamp and Childress, 2001; Hall et al., 2012; Prichard et al., 2014).
non-maleficence
This principle necessitates that the researchers min-
imise the risk of harm to the research participants. This may be direct as well
as indirect harm (such as embarrassment or social stigmatisation) (Brody,
1998; Hall et al., 2012; Prichard et al., 2014)
5
==PAGE:26==
6
literature review
beneficence
This principle entails that the conducted research can re-
sult in beneﬁts for the research participants or society that signiﬁcantly out-
weighs risks associated with research participation (Beauchamp and Chil-
dress, 2001; Hall et al., 2012; Prichard et al., 2014)
distributive justice
This principle stipulates that both the burdens
and the beneﬁts of the research study are evenly and fairly distributed
among the research participants (no groups are singled out) (Brody, 1998;
Hall et al., 2012; Burgard et al., 2014).
Hall et al. (2012) concluded that for large populations, wastewater analysis
(at the inlet to the WWTP) does not raise any major ethical issues. Moreover,
the authors concluded that it would be impossible to single out the individ-
ual drug user. Prichard et al. (2014) found that all of the above principles
except distributive justice are pertinent to the ethical discussion of sewage
epidemiology. The ethical assessment of any research study necessitates the
weighing of the aforementioned principles.
If sewage epidemiology is used to monitor the trends in illicit drug use
from large catchments, Hall et al. (2012) ﬁnd that it does not raise major
ethical issues, as any of the four principles are not violated to great extent.
With regard to respect for autonomy, it would be impossible to obtain the
informed consent from the population present in the catchment, however,
this is negated by the conﬁdentiality assured by the fact that the wastewater
is a pooled urine sample from the whole catchment. The individual contri-
bution can as such not be identiﬁed. No participants are directly harmed
by the analysis, however, the possibility of stigmatisation of a given area
based on the measured/estimated illicit drug consumption is a real risk
(Hall et al., 2012; Prichard et al., 2014). Prichard et al. (2014) argues that
both the size of the population served by the WWTP and the location of the
WWTP be anonymised to abate the issue of stigmatisation. The authors also
highlight the importance of proper dissemination of the research conclusion
and that the researchers have an important responsibility to reduce the risk
of incorrect media communication (Prichard et al., 2014). The principle of
beneﬁcence is satisﬁed as the wastewater analysis is a non-intrusive way to
estimate illicit drug use as well as trend (Daughton, 2001; Hall et al., 2012).
This can be used to evaluate the effectiveness of a given drug prevention
policy and allow for the optimisation and change hereof (Hall et al., 2012).
The principle of distributive justice is also observed as no groups are sin-
gled out in a large catchment, and the drug user may receive the beneﬁts
of such a study (increased funding for addiction treatment etc.). However,
as argued by Prichard et al. (2014), the media coverage associated with the
publication of sewage epidemiology studies may also result in politically in-
duced changes to the current law enforcement practices (the need to “...’get
tough’ on drug use and crime.”), as this is normally a popular political
subject (Prichard et al., 2014). In that regard, the principle of distributive
justice may be violated, as more drug users may be prosecuted and the
punitive measures may be increased (higher sentences for what was pre-
viously misdemeanours), and the marginalised areas be disproportionately
affected (Prichard et al., 2014).
==PAGE:27==
2.1 ethical aspects of wastewater analysis and sewage epidemiology
7
For special venues and site-speciﬁc studies, such as entertainment venues,
prisons, and schools, the associated ethical issues are more complex (Hall et
al., 2012; Prichard et al., 2014). In the following paragraphs, different studies
and the ethical issues of these will be presented.
Hall et al. (2012) argues that as drug use in prisons constitutes a risk for
both the inmates and the prison staff, wastewater analysis can have positive
implications for the safety and health of both inmates and staff. The wastew-
ater analysis could also assist in the evaluation of current anti-drug policies
enforced by the prison facility. This is however only true if the monitoring is
not limited to individual cell blocks where identiﬁcation of the user is pos-
sible. However, Hall et al. (2012) ﬁnds that the possible policy responses by
the authorities may have negative implications for inmates not partaking in
drug use (e.g. by eliminating contact visits by relatives) – i.e. collective puni-
tive measures due to drug use by individuals (such actions are not unique
to wastewater analysis but may also be introduced based on drug seizures
or overdose deaths). Dyken et al. (2014a) and Dyken et al. (2014b) analysed
the wastewater in a small prison with approximately 400 inmates (of both
sexes), and concluded that the sewage epidemiology and wastewater analy-
sis are valuable tools to monitor the drug consumption, however they note
that much care must be taken when analysing the use by a small group.
Katsoyiannis and Jones (2011) proposed to install autosamplers in the sew-
erage system near the residential blocks in the olympic village in order to
provide the anti-doping agencies with a strategic advantage in their strug-
gle against doping. Harman et al. (2011a) commended the work done by
Katsoyiannis and Jones (2011), however they do not consider the practical
approach feasible. Instead, they suggest that the only technique which may
be feasible is to use passive samplers (Harman et al., 2011b; Harman et al.,
2011a). This will however be in direct violation of the principle of autonomy
since it would be sampling without consent (Burgard et al., 2014). However,
the olympic athletes (and athletes in general) are already subject to controls
at random. This could make the application of this method in observance
with the ethical principles presented, as the athletes already have consented
to blood tests at random. As such, the presence of passive samplers may
very well be considered as an extension of this. However, to successfully
attribute the presence of an illicit compound on a passive sampler to the
single individual will most likely prove to be impossible in practice.
Analyses done on wastewater from schools and colleges have also been pub-
lished (Panawennage et al., 2011; Burgard et al., 2013). Roche et al. (2009)
and Hall et al. (2012) note that this allows for the stigmatisation of the
school and all the students. To avoid this, the wastewater analysis could
be done in a manner similar to the currently employed school surveys in
which the schools are chosen at random (Hall et al., 2012). Alternatively, the
data could be completely anonymised and the data only used for targeted
anti-drug campaigns.
Li et al. (2014) sampled directly from a sewage pipe emanating from an
area in Beijing renowned for it’s nightclubs. Archer et al. (2013) pooled the
urine from portable toilets (mainly for male use). These examples show that
even though the samples still are per se anonymous, the sampling occurs
further upstream than proposed originally by Daughton (2001). Coupled
==PAGE:28==
8
literature review
with other means of surveillance and registration (e.g. surveillance cameras,
credit card transactions, GPS logging and surveillance of mobile phones,
and metadata in general), it could be possible to identify the individuals
partaking in illegal drug use or the individuals frequenting venues in which
illicit drug consumption is widespread. In general, the ethical issues related
to wastewater analysis and the political policies that may be changed or
introduced based on these results are even more pertinent when analyses
and studies are conducted in countries with poor respect for human rights
(Prichard et al., 2014).
Essentially, the ethical issues associated with sewage epidemiology are in-
trinsically associated with the purpose of the study – is it used to under-
stand the underlying trends in (illicit) drug use and used to abate and target
prevention campaigns for certain drugs, or is it used as a tool for punitive
measures and surveillance of users (Burgard et al., 2014). Prichard et al.
(2014) concluded that formal sewage epidemiology research ethics are not
required but the ethical risks should instead be managed by developing a
guideline document. The document is then intended for both the researchers
conducting the studies as well as the reviewers and publishers.
Based on this brief overview and examples of ethical issues, the ethical im-
plications of this present study are thought to be negligible. No studies
regarding consumption trends of a speciﬁc catchment is undertaken. The
wastewater used in the analysis is however derived from at real catchment,
and the concentration of drugs is determined before the addition (spiking)
of the drugs of interest. However, the WWTP serves more than 100,000 in-
habitants, and this research merely focuses on the in-sewer transformation
of different illicit drugs, for which reason the knowledge of the background
concentration is mainly used to properly determine the initial concentration
of the drugs investigated.
2.2
physicochemical,
pharmacological,
and
pharmacoki-
netic data
The data presented herein are listed under the parent compound. Aside
from the most relevant physicochemical properties, the metabolic pathway
and the degradation pathway are also described where information was
available. The information presented in the following is not meant to be
exhaustive.
2.2.1
Methamphetamine
Methamphetamine is a N-methyl derivative of amphetamine (Melis et al.,
2011). Besides recreational use it is also used medically. As the majority of
the investigated drugs, methamphetamine is a chiral drug with different
potencies and applications associated with the different enantiomer. The
R(-)-methamphetamine is less potent and is used medically in nasal inhaler
decongestant products (Huestis and Cone, 2007). Only in the USA does
the nasal decongestant Vick’s Vapor inhaler contain R(-)methamphetamine
==PAGE:29==
2.2 physicochemical, pharmacological, and pharmacokinetic data
9
(Li et al., 2010). S(+)-methamphetamine has been used to treat attention
deﬁcit disorder and obesity (Huestis and Cone, 2007). Illicit manufacturing
of methamphetamine in clandestine laboratories results in a relative pure
production of S(+)-methamphetamine (Huestis and Cone, 2007). Moreover,
R(-)-methamphetamine can also be formed by metabolism of prescription
medicine (selegiline used to treat depression, early stage Parkinson’s disease
etc.) (Kasprzyk-Hordern and Baker, 2012).
2.2.1.1
Physicochemical properties
The physicochemical properties of methamphetamine listed in Table 1 show
that it is ionised at environmentally relevant pH. It is a base and the pKa
was found to be 9.9-10.1 (Moffat et al., 2011). The octanol-water partitioning
coefﬁcient (logKow) show that it could possibly partition onto hydrophobic
suspended matter.

2.2.1.2
Pharmacokinetics
Methamphetamine can be smoked, nasally insufﬂated, orally ingested, or
injected intravenously (Schepers et al., 2003; Huestis and Cone, 2007; Melis
et al., 2011). All routes allow for rapid absorption (Schepers et al., 2003;
Huestis and Cone, 2007). Methamphetamine is primarily metabolised in the
liver (Huestis and Cone, 2007) and is predominantly excreted with urine
(Schepers et al., 2003; Shima et al., 2006).

methamphetamine

hydroxymethamphetamine
(p-OHMA).

by
isoenzyme CYP2D6 (Lin et al., 1997). Methamphetamine can also be hy-
droxylated to N-hydroxymethamphetamine (Li et al., 2010). Amphetamine
can be metabolised further, as will be discussed later. The phase II
metabolism results in addition of a glucuronide or sulphate moiety to
p-OHMA (Shima et al., 2006). The metabolism in man can be seen in Figure
1.
Li et al. (2010) found that methamphetamine as well as amphetamine and
p-OHMA could be found in urine for at least 24 hours after being in-
==PAGE:30==
10
literature review
travenously administrated. Moreover, p-OHMA was found to be excreted
at a higher percentage than amphetamine (Shima et al., 2006; Li et al.,
2010). In the same study it was concluded that the metabolism of metham-
phetamine is enatioselective as more R(-)-methamphetamine was excreted
than S(+)-methamphetamine, possibly ascribed to a lower conversion of
R(-)-methamphetamine to R(-)-amphetamine and R(-)-p-OHMA. The excre-
tion of methamphetamine and amphetamine in urine is pH dependent
(Beckett and Rowland, 1965). Li et al., 2010 suggest that p-OHMA may be a
more stable biomarker of methamphetamine abuse as their ﬁndings indicate
that the metabolic pathway is less stereoselective.
Figure 1: Metabolism of methamphetamine in man. Adapted from Shima et al.
(2006).
2.2.1.3
Degradation pathways
Bagnall et al. (2013) investigated the transformation of methamphetamine
in river microcosms. The results showed that the transformation of metham-
phetamine is biologically mediated and stereoselective. The S(+)-enantiomer
was preferably biotransformed. Experiments also revealed that sorption has
a limited impact on both transformation and stereoselectivity (no signif-
icant difference was observed between abiotic bioreactors with or with-
out suspended sediment) (Bagnall et al., 2013). The addition of sediment
to bioreactors exposed to light accelerated the degradation (increased the
degradation rate) despite the fact that methamphetamine was shown to
be photostable (Bagnall et al., 2013). The authors hypothesise that it may
be due to a higher microbial abundance and diversity. It was moreover
shown, using deuterated analogues, that methamphetamine-d14 was bio-
transformed to amphetamine-d11 in both light and dark bioreactors, and
methamphetamine was stereoselective biodegraded as the S(+)-enantiomer
was preferentially transformed, hence leading to a R(-)-methamphtamine
and S(+)-amphetamine enrichment (Bagnall et al., 2013).
Bagnall et al. (2013) build on the ﬁndings made in Kasprzyk-Hordern
and Baker (2012) and Kasprzyk-Hordern et al. (2010). Kasprzyk-Hordern
and Baker (2012) found methamphetamine to be subject to stereoselec-
tive removal by WWTPs. The efﬂuent was found to be enriched in
R(-)-methamphetamine Baker and Kasprzyk-Hordern (2011) found metham-
phetamine to be less stable in unﬁltered wastewater than in ﬁltered wastew-
ater. Nuijs et al. (2012) found methamptamine to be stable in raw wastewater.
==PAGE:31==
2.2 physicochemical, pharmacological, and pharmacokinetic data
11
Subedi and Kannan (2014) found that methamphetamine does not readily
partition onto suspended particulate matter.
2.2.2
Amphetamine
Amphetamine is a synthetic phenethylamine derivative (Melis et al., 2011)
and a chiral compound (Bagnall et al., 2013). Amphetamine is not solely
used as a recreational drug but is also used to treat narcolepsy, obesity,
and attention deﬁcit disorder (Ellenhorn et al., 1997; Melis et al., 2011).
Amphetamine is also a metabolite of not only methamphetamine degrada-
tion but also other drugs or prescription medications (Moffat et al., 2011;
Kasprzyk-Hordern and Baker, 2012), e.g. selegiline used to treat depression,
early stage Parkinson’s disease, leads to the formation of R(-)-amphetamine
(Kasprzyk-Hordern and Baker, 2012). Amphetamine is mainly consumed
orally (Melis et al., 2011).
2.2.2.1
Physicochemical properties
The physicochemical properties of amphetamine list in Table 2 show that
it is ionised at environmentally relevant pH. It is a base and the pKa was
found to be 9.3-10.1 (Moffat et al., 2011). The logKow show that it could
possibly partition onto suspended matter.

2.2.2.2
Pharmacokinetics
The chirality of amphetamine leads to different psychoactive stimulat-
ing activities in the two enantiomers (Kasprzyk-Hordern et al., 2010).
S(+)-amphetamine is ﬁve time as potent as R(-)-amphetamine (Herráez-
Hernández et al., 2002). Hence, amphetamine used reacreationally is pre-
dominately the (S)(+)-enantiomer (if possible), else the consumption is of
the racemic mixture (Melis et al., 2011).
==PAGE:32==
12
literature review
Amphetamine is in the human metabolism excreted in the urine and
the metabolism is mainly mediated by Cytochrome P450 (CYP) isoen-
zyme CYP2C (Kraemer and Maurer, 2002). The phase I reactions entail
oxidative deaminination to form phenylacetone, aromatic hydroxylation to
form 4-hydroxyamphetamine, β-hydroxylation to form norphedrine, and N-
oxidation to form a hydroxylamine derivative (cf. Figure 2) (Santagati et al.,
2002; Moffat et al., 2011; Kraemer and Maurer, 2002). The β-hydroxylation to
form norphedrine has been shown to be stereoselective for the S(+)-isomer
(Moffat et al., 2011)
The phase II reactions entail the addition of water soluble moieties, and the
products from phase I are excreted as glucuronide and/or sulphate conju-
gates (Kraemer and Maurer, 2002; Santagati et al., 2002; Moffat et al., 2011).
The urinary pH has implications for the distribution of excreted metabolites.
In acidic urine, the concentration of amphetamine is increased (Moffat et al.,
2011). At neutral pH roughly 30% of the dose is excreted as amphetamine af-
ter 24 hours. At acidic pH approx. 74% of the administered dose is excreted
as amphetamine (Moffat et al., 2011). In alkaline urine it may decrease to
1%-4% (Moffat et al., 2011).
Figure 2: Human metabolism of amphetamine adapted from Santagati et al. (2002).
2.2.2.3
Degradation pathways
After excretion from the human body, the metabolites are subject to fur-
ther (re)transformation in the environment. Bagnall et al. (2013) showed bio-
transformation of amphetamine in river microcosms as well as stereoselec-
tive biodegradation, where the S(+)-isomer is preferably transformed by the
biomass. The addition of suspended matter further enhanced the biotrans-
formation of amphetamine. The removal due to sorption did not appear to
be a signiﬁcant removal process (Bagnall et al., 2013). The addition of sedi-
ment to bioreactors exposed to light accelerated the degradation (increased
==PAGE:33==
2.2 physicochemical, pharmacological, and pharmacokinetic data
13
the degradation rate) despite the fact that amphetamine was shown to be
photostable (Bagnall et al., 2013). The authors hypothesise that it may be
due to a higher microbial abundance and diversity. In the same study, the
transformation of methamphetamine to amphetamine was shown (Bagnall
et al., 2013). Nuijs et al. (2012) found amphetamine to be stable in wastewater
at pH 7.5 and 20◦C. Subedi and Kannan (2014) found that 40.1% of the mass
of amphetamine was sorbed to suspended matter in the inﬂuent, which is
considerably higher than what they found in their literature review.
2.2.3
Cocaine
Cocaine is an alkaloid obtained either from the dried leaves of Erythroxylum
coca (and other Erythroxylum species) or synthesised from ecgonine (Moffat
et al., 2011). Cocaine is one of the most used illicit drugs in North America,
western and central Europe, and South America (Melis et al., 2011). As noted
in the introduction, Zuccato et al. (2005) targeted cocaine when they applied
the approach suggested by Daughton (2001). Aside from reacreational use,
cocaine was historically used as an anaesthetic and analgesic and is still
applied topically as an anaesthetic in eye and nasal surgery (Melis et al.,
2011; Moffat et al., 2011).

Physicochemical properties

In Table 3 the physicochemical data of the investigated compounds related
to the metabolism of cocaine are shown. All compounds have basic proper-
ties (Moffat et al., 2011).
Benzoylecgonine has two pKas as it is a zwitterion. It will essentially be com-
pletely ionised at environmentally relevant pH (Domènech et al., 2009). The
same would be expected for cocaethylene, whereas cocaine and ecgonine
methyl ester predominately will be ionised, however a considerable fraction
will be nonionised at environmentally relevant albeit high pH.
==PAGE:34==
14
literature review
2.2.3.2
Pharmacokinetics
Cocaine hydrochloride is the chemical form mainly used for nasal insufﬂa-
tion, ingestion, and intravenous adminstration. Cocaine can also be found
as free base and is usually smoked (Khan and Nicell, 2011).
Cocaine is predominately metabolised by three different esterases (Maurer
et al., 2006) (cited in Melis et al. (2011)). In the liver, cocaine is hydrolysed by
human carboxylesterase-1 (hCE-1) to benzoylecgonine (Bencharit et al., 2003;
Moffat et al., 2011). In the blood or the plasma cocaine is hydrolysed to ecgo-
nine methyl ester by pseudocholinesterase-2 and human carboxylesterase-
2 (hCE-2) (Inaba, 1989; Laizure et al., 2002; Bencharit et al., 2003; Maurer
et al., 2006). Cocaine is also N-demethylated to norcocaine, however, this
only accounts for 5% of the total adsorbed dose (Melis et al., 2011; Mof-
fat et al., 2011). Other reported metabolic products are: ecgonine, ethylec-
gonine, hydroxycocaine, methylecgonidine (anhydroecgonine methyl ester),
norbenzoylecgonine, and norecgonine (Kolbrich et al., 2006; Moffat et al.,
2011; Melis et al., 2011; Khan and Nicell, 2011; Bisceglia and Lippa, 2014).
When cocaine is used concomitantly with ethanol, cocaethylene is formed
by transesteriﬁcation catalysed by hCE-1 (Laizure et al., 2002; Bencharit et
al., 2003).
When administered intravenously (a daily dose of 120 mg), 1-9% of the dose
is excreted unchanged as cocaine in the urine, and 35%-55% is excreted as
benzoylecgonine (Melis et al., 2011). When the urine is acidic, the fraction
of cocaine increases (Moffat et al., 2011). Ecgonine methyl ester constitutes
30%-60% of the excreted mass when cocaine is either nasally insufﬂated,
orally ingested, or intravenously administered. The smoking of cocaine re-
sults in unique pyrolysis products excreted in the urine which can be used
to determine this route of administration.
The data compiled by Khan and Nicell (2011) suggest that the excretion
proﬁles when cocaine is administered orally, intravenously, or nasally are
similar. Signiﬁcantly lower amounts are observed to be excreted when co-
caine is smoked mainly ascribed to vaporisation and pyrolysis products left
in the smoking device. For all administration routes, the major excretion
product is benzoylecgonine, then ecgonine methyl ester, and lastly cocaine.
A fraction of the dose is also excreted through faeces (Jeffcoat et al., 1989)
(cited in Khan and Nicell (2011)). The half-life of cocaine is relatively short,
and has been found to be 0.5 hr - 1.1 hr for intravenously administered co-
caine, and 0.9 hr to 1.5 hr when nasally insufﬂated or orally ingested (Inaba,
1989).
2.2.3.3
Degradation pathways
Many recent studies have focused on the fate of cocaine in sewer systems,
e.g. Zuccato et al. (2005), Nuijs et al. (2009), Banta-Green et al. (2009), Cas-
tiglioni et al. (2011), Plósz et al. (2013b), and Bisceglia and Lippa (2014).
Britt et al. (1992) identiﬁed a cocaine esterase in bacteria that was able to
hydrolyse cocaine to ecgonine methyl ester. Lister et al. (1996) showed that
a mixed bacterial culture was able to utilise cocaine as the sole source of
==PAGE:35==
2.2 physicochemical, pharmacological, and pharmacokinetic data
15
energy and carbon transforming it to ecgonine methyl ester, and further
to ecgonine among others. The bacteria were isolated by selective enrich-
ment using samples taken from a cocaine-processing unit. Hence, cocaine
has been shown to be biotransformed in a (adapted) natural system due to
the biogenic nature of the compound (Lister et al., 1996).
Figure 3: Proposed hydrolysis of cocaine and the metabolites targeted in this thesis.
Adapted from Bisceglia and Lippa (2014).
Khan and Nicell (2011) suggest to use benzoylecgonine as the biomarker
for cocaine consumption due to its apparent stability as it has been shown
not to be readily biotransformed nor sorp to suspended matter. Zuccato et
al. (2008) reached the same conclusion. While cocaethylene is only excreted
when cocaine is used concomitantly with ethanol, the use of this compound
as a biomarker is inhibited by the missing assessment of its degradation
potential (Khan and Nicell, 2011).
Gheorghe et al. (2008) showed that both benzoylecgonine and cocaine ex-
hibit high stability when stored at -20◦C, 4◦C, and 20◦C (ﬁve days at pH 2
for both compounds, and pH 6 for benzoylecgonine). However, Castiglioni
et al. (2006) showed that when stored at 4◦C for three days, the cocaine con-
centration decreases 36% and the benzoylecgonine concentration increases
14%. Moreover, the cocaethylene concentration also decreased 13%. These
stabilities were assessed for samples without pH adjustment prior to stor-
age. Gheorghe et al. (2008) also showed that after ﬁve hours storage in ambi-
ent temperature at pH 6, almost 40% of cocaine and ecgonine methyl ester
would disappear followed by an increase of benzoylecgonine. After 24 hours
almost 90% of cocaine and ecgonine methyl ester had been degraded. Re-
search and experiments have shown that cocaine and its metabolites are
not expected to partition onto solids in the wastewater (Gheorghe et al.,
2008; Domènech et al., 2009; Baker and Kasprzyk-Hordern, 2011; Plósz et
al., 2013b).
Domènech et al. (2009) used EPI suite to estimate the hydroxyl radical reac-
tion rate and obtained a value of 0.29 hr−1 for benzoylecgonine and cocaine.
Moreover, spontaneous hydrolysis of cocaine to benzoylecgonine occurs at
==PAGE:36==
16
literature review
alkaline pH (Moffat et al., 2011). As cocaine readily hydrolyses, it can be ex-
pected to readily degrade in the sewer system (Gheorghe et al., 2008; Khan
and Nicell, 2011; Plósz et al., 2013b; Bisceglia and Lippa, 2014). Both Plósz
et al. (2013b) and Bisceglia and Lippa (2014) were successful in applying
a hydrolysis model (cf. Figure 3) to their experimental data, however, they
disagree on the magnitude of the rate constants, where Plósz et al. (2013b)
obtained rates higher than was obtained by Bisceglia and Lippa (2014). The
differences may be attributable to the different experimental procedures, dif-
ferent microbial ecologies in the wastewater (Bisceglia and Lippa, 2014) or
differences in pH. Senta et al. (2014) found that the transformation of co-
caine to benzoylecgonine was mainly due to chemical hydrolysis.
2.2.4
Heroin
Morphine and its derivative heroin (3,6-diacetylmorphine, diamorphine),
and codein are all opioids (plant alkaloids). Morphine and codeine are
present naturally in opium (which derives from the dried milk juice of un-
ripe poppy seed pods) (Boerner et al., 1975). Heroin was synthesised as
a morphine substitute while searching for a less toxic and less addictive
derivative (it was ﬁrst synthesised in 1874) (Boerner et al., 1975). However,
heroin has a higher toxicity and addictiveness than morphine (Boerner et
al., 1975; Moffat et al., 2011) and is considered one of the most dangerous
drugs of abuse (Melis et al., 2011). Heroin abuse and addiction was seen to
increase after the ﬁrst world war and again from 1968 (Boerner et al., 1975).
Morphine and codeine are commonly used as pain medication. In general
opioids are readily used as analgesics (Smith, 2009).
Heroin can enter the body by nasal insufﬂation, intravenous or subcuta-
neous injection, or inhalation (“chasing”) (Boerner et al., 1975; Moffat et al.,
2011; Melis et al., 2011). The mode of administration has implications for
the excretion pattern of the heroin and its metabolites (Boerner et al., 1975;
Moffat et al., 2011; Melis et al., 2011). Injection of heroin is the preferred
route of administration, however, chasing has also been reported as a pre-
ferred route of administration by addicts undergoing treatment (EMCDDA,
2015b).
2.2.4.1
Physicochemical properties
Table 4 shows the physicochemical data for heroin and its metabolites. For
all of the compounds, partitioning onto suspended matter should not be ex-
pected to occur to any great extent. The pKas suggest that the compounds
are present as both ionised and neutral species at environmentally relevant
pH. Morphine-3-β-D-glucuronide has zwitterionic properties, and the val-
ues are for the strongest acidic functional group and strongest basic func-
tional group.

2.2.4.2
Pharmacokinetics
The phase I metabolism of opiods is predominately mediated by cytochrome
P450 (CYP) isoenzymes in the liver. The phase II metabolism is mediated
by uridine diphosphate glucuronosyltransferases (predominately UGT2B7)
(Boerner et al., 1975; Smith, 2009).
Upon administration and introduction into the human body, heroin
is rapidly deacetylated to 6-(mono)acetylmorphine (6-MAM) by blood
esterases
present
in
the
blood
(hCE-1,
hCE-2,
and
serum
butyryl-
cholinesterase) (Bencharit et al., 2003; Moffat et al., 2011). In the liver, 6-
MAM is further hydrolysed to morphine (Smith, 2009). Morphine does
not undergo phase I metabolism but undergoes glucuronidation via
UGT2B7 (Smith, 2009). The glucuronidation mainly results in two differ-
ent major metabolites: morphine-6-glucuronide (M6G) and morphine-3-β-
D-glucuronide (M3G). It may however also undergo other minor routes of
metabolism such as N-demethylation to normorphine (Smith, 2009).
Codeine has to be metabolised to morphine in the body in order to exert its
analgesic effect. Codeine is O-dealkylated to morphine (Smith, 2009; Moffat
et al., 2011). Norcodeine is a major metabolite of codeine and is excreted in
the urine after use of codeine (Boerner et al., 1975). Medical use of codeine
will result in excretion of both codeine and morphine in the urine (Wolff
et al., 1999). Codeine is also excreted as codeine-6-glucuronide (Boerner et
al., 1975; Moffat et al., 2011), and has also been found to be metabolised
to hydrocodone (Smith, 2009). The majority of a codeine dose is excreted
as codeine-6-glucuronide, then norcodeine, and then morphine (Melis et al.,
2011). The metabolism of heroin is shown in Figure 4.
Up to 80% of an administered heroin dose is excreted in the urine within
24 hours (Moffat et al., 2011). The excretion products are mainly conjugated
morphine metabolites, morphine (5-7%), 6-MAM (1%), heroin (0.1%), and
trace amounts of other metabolite (Moffat et al., 2011). If 6-MAM is detected
==PAGE:38==
18
literature review
Figure 4: Metabolism and major metabolites of heroin in man based on work done
or compiled by Boerner et al. (1975), Wolff et al. (1999), Smith (2009), Moffat et al.
(2011), and Melis et al. (2011).
in the urine, this is generally considered to be indicative of heroin consump-
tion (Wolff et al., 1999). The route of administration has a large effect on the
excretion rates and proﬁles (Boerner et al., 1975; Khan and Nicell, 2011).
2.2.4.3
Degradation pathways
Khan and Nicell (2011) did a thorough review of the available literature in
order to recommend a biomarker to be used for estimation of heroin con-
sumption in a catchment. The licit consumption of morphine and codeine
has a profound effect on the morphine concentrations excreted into the
sewer systems in North American countries and Scandinavia. Moreover,
the possibility to acquire codeine formulations as over-the-counter drugs
in Scandinavian countries further complicates the possible use of morphine
as the sole biomarker for heroin abuse. However, for other European coun-
==PAGE:39==
2.2 physicochemical, pharmacological, and pharmacokinetic data
19
tries, morphine is suggested to be useful as a biomarker (Khan and Nicell,
2011). Instead 6-MAM can be used in countries in which the endogenous
morphine concentration due to licit routes are believed to be predominant
in the sewer systems (Khan and Nicell, 2011).
Nuijs et al. (2012) observed a 20% decrease of 6-MAM after 12 hours in
wastewater at 20◦C and pH 7.5. Baker and Kasprzyk-Hordern (2011) found
heroin to be readily degraded when stored at pH 7.4 at 2◦C, and 19◦C in raw
wastewater. 80% was removed after 12 hours at 19◦C. Similarly, 6-MAM was
also found to readily be degraded, however not to the same extent as heroin.
Under the same conditions, the codeine concentration increased (Baker and
Kasprzyk-Hordern, 2011). M3G was also found to be readily transformed
to morphine under the test conditions: after 12 hours at 19◦C almost 85% of
M3G was transformed (a concomitant increase of morphine was found to
be roughly 50%) (Baker and Kasprzyk-Hordern, 2011).
2.2.5
Ketamine
Ketamine has been used as an anaesthetic in both humans and animals since
the 1970s while also being used recreationally (White et al., 1982; Doston
et al., 1995; Lin and Lua, 2004; Turfus et al., 2009). Ketamine is a chiral
compound where S(+)-ketamine is more potent than R(-)-ketamine (Doston
et al., 1995; Ihmsen and Schiittler, 2001)

The physicochemical properties of ketamine and norketamine are shown in
Table 5. Both compounds show a somewhat high octanol-water distribution
coefﬁcient, hence, partitioning onto solid matter should be expected. The
pKa of both compounds show that they are present at both ionised and
neutral species at environmentally relevant pH, however, at higher pH they
are predominately found as neutral species (both compounds act like bases).
==PAGE:40==
20
literature review
2.2.5.2
Pharmacokinetics
Ketamine is metabolised in the liver. 72 hours after administration 90% of
the dose has been excreted in the urine (Lin and Lua, 2004; Moffat et al.,
2011). Ketamine is phase I metabolised to norketamine and dehydronorke-
tamine in the liver by cytochrome P450 isoenzymes (Lin and Lua, 2004).
Of the metabolites, approximately 2% is the unchanged drug, 2% is norke-
tamine, 16% is dehydronorketamine, and the remaining 80% are conjugated
hydroxylated metabolites due to phase II metabolism (Lin and Lua, 2004;
Moffat et al., 2011). The metabolism of ketamine is steroeselective where the
S(+)-enantiomer is more readily excreted (Ihmsen and Schiittler, 2001).
2.2.5.3
Degradation pathways
Lin et al. (2014) investigated the microbial degradation of ketamine and nor-
ketamine in surface water and found no degradation during the experiment
(30 days). Both ketamine and norketamine are expected to be stable in both
raw and ﬁltered raw wastewater at 19◦C and pH 7.4 when stored for three
days (Baker and Kasprzyk-Hordern, 2011).
2.2.6
MDMA
MDMA and its metabolite MDA are both ring-substituted amphetamine
analogues (Kasprzyk-Hordern and Baker, 2012). MDMA is solely a recre-
ational drug and has as of yet no medical applications (Kasprzyk-Hordern
and Baker, 2012). MDMA is a chiral compound which has implications for
biological systems as these may be subject to enantiomeric stereoselectivity
(Kasprzyk-Hordern and Baker, 2012).
MDA can also be acquired and used as a recreational drug. Hence, the
presence of MDA in the environment can be due to use of MDMA or direct
use of MDA (Kasprzyk-Hordern and Baker, 2012). Moreover, MDA is also
a metabolite of MDEA (methylendioxyethylamphetamine) which has also
seen recreational use (Melis et al., 2011).
2.2.6.1
Physicochemical properties
The physicochemical properties of MDMA and MDA are listed in Table 6.
Both compounds have logKow that suggest the possibility for partitioning
onto particulate matter. Their pKas are rather high, and they can both be
expected to be ionised at environmentally relevant pH as they have basic
properties.

2.2.6.2
Pharmacokinetics
MDMA is excreted in urine. Approximately 65% of the dose is excreted
as MDMA within 3 days (Moffat et al., 2011). Following N-demethylation,
7% is excreted as 3,4-methylenedioxyamphetamine (MDA), which is
transformed further via O-demethylation to 3,4-dihydroxyamphetamine
(HHA). MDMA is also O-demethylated to 3,4-dihydroxymethamphetamine
(HHMA). HHMA and HHA are then O-methylated by catechol-O-
methyltransferase to 4-hydroxy-3-methoxymethamphetamine (HMMA) and
4-hydroxy-3-methoxyamphetamine (HMA), respectively (Moffat et al., 2011).
The phase II metabolism entails that HHMA, HHA, HMMA, and HMA are
excreted as the conjugated glucuronide and sulphate metabolites (Moffat
et al., 2011; De La Torre et al., 2000; Schwaninger et al., 2012; Maurer et al.,
2000). The metabolic pathways are shown in Figure 5.
MDMA is a chiral compound and has been shown to be subject to
stereoselectivity in the human metabolism (Schwaninger et al., 2012). In
Schwaninger et al. (2012), racemic doses of MDMA were administered to 10
participants and the urinary concentration of phase I and phase II metabo-
lites were measured. The analyses revealed that MDA was only present in
low concentrations in the urine. The study also showed, that the urinary re-
coveries of the R(-)-enantiomers of MDMA, HHMA, HMMA sulphate were
higher. The same was observed for S(+)-enantiomers of HMMA and HMMA
glucuronide. However, for MDA and HHMA sulphate enantiomers no sig-
niﬁcantly difference in selectivity was observed (Schwaninger et al., 2012).
2.2.6.3
Degradation pathways
Kasprzyk-Hordern and Baker (2012) found MDMA in raw wastewater to
be enriched with R(-)-MDMA. The wastewater treatment was also found to
result in an enrichment of R(-)-MDMA in the efﬂuent (Kasprzyk-Hordern
and Baker, 2012). Enrichment was found to be higher in the efﬂuent of treat-
ment plants employing activated sludge compared to treatment plants us-
ing trickling ﬁlters (Kasprzyk-Hordern and Baker, 2012). The authors sug-
gest that the enrichment is due to enantioselective biotransformation and
the difference between the treatment methods reﬂects the ability of differ-
ent microbial consortia (Kasprzyk-Hordern and Baker, 2012). Moreover, the
==PAGE:42==
22
literature review
Figure 5: Human metabolism of MDMA. Adapted from De La Torre et al. (2000) and
Lanz et al. (1997).
authors also investigated the presence in the receiving water and found the
enrichment to increase when sampling further downstream. This was also
observed to be more pronounced during summer than during winter which
may be ascribed to different microbiological activity (Kasprzyk-Hordern
and Baker, 2012). Thai et al. (2014) found MDMA to be stable under both
aerobic and anaerobic sewer conditions.
Like MDMA, MDA is also subject to stereoselective metabolism. Similarly
to S(+)-MDMA, S(+)-MDA is preferentially metabolised leading to enrich-
ment of R(-)-MDA as excretion product (Meyer et al., 2009). As noted by
Kasprzyk-Hordern and Baker (2012), the consumption of MDMA will re-
sult in an increased amount of S(+)-MDA in urine. This was also the result
of the investigations done by said authors. At the inﬂuent to a treatment
plant, the raw wastewater was enriched in S(+)-MDA, which is said to de-
rive from MDMA consumption and metabolism. After treatment the efﬂu-
ent was shown to be enriched in R(-)-MDA indicating stereoselectivity in
the treatment process (Kasprzyk-Hordern and Baker, 2012). In Kasprzyk-
Hordern et al. (2010), the MDMA concentration was also observed to be
lower in the efﬂuent than in the inﬂuent after biological treatment suggest-
ing the possibility of its further transformation or sorption.
2.2.7
Mephedrone
Mephedrone is a synthetic cathinone. Cathinone is the active compound in
the leaves of the khat plant (Catha edulis) (Carroll et al., 2012). The com-
pound has a structure similar to that of (meth)amphetamine and MDMA as
it is a substituted β-keto amphetamine (Green et al., 2014). Due to the in-
volvement of the substance in unexplained deaths in the UK, mephedrone
gained substantial media attention and was subsequently classiﬁed as a con-
trolled substance in the European countries in December 2010 (Green et al.,
2014; EMCDDA, 2011)
==PAGE:43==

The physicochemical data for mephedrone that was possible to obtain is
listed in Table 7. Not much could be found, however, the estimated logKow
suggest that it may partition onto particulate matter. The compound is more-
over present as ionic and neutral species at environmentally relevant pH.
2.2.7.2
Pharmacokinetics
Mephedrone is mainly ingested orally or administered through nasal in-
sufﬂation by recreational users (Green et al., 2014). Meyer et al. (2010) pro-
posed that mephedrone has ﬁve different phase I metabolites. Mephedrone
is metabolised via N-demethylation to either nor-hydroxytolylmephedrone
and nor-mephedrone. These are in turn further transformed (only two of
the ﬁve metabolites are included in Fig. 6, which illustrates the metabolic
pathways of mephedrone). Three distinct pathways were suggested: “N-
demethylation to the primary amine, reduction of the keto moiety to the
respective alcohol, and oxidation of the tolyl moiety to the corresponding
alcohols.” (Meyer et al., 2010)
2.2.7.3
Degradation pathways
The literature review did not yield any results when searching for exper-
imental data suggesting environmental biodegradation. However, Reid et
al. (2014b) used the SMARTCyp tool to predict the degradation pathway
of Cytochrome P450 metabolism. The results predicted metabolites similar
to the ones proposed in Meyer et al. (2010). Interestingly, the research con-
ducted by Reid et al. (2014b) also revealed that mephedrone was less stable
in sewage at room temperature (21 ◦C). Only 64% of the spiked mass re-
mained after 24 hours. If this instability was due to sorption or biodegrada-
tion is not clear.

metabolites as proposed by Meyer et al. (2010), adapted from Green et al. (2014).
2.2.8
Methadone
Methadone is used as an analgesic as well as for maintenance treatment
of opioid addicts (Sullivan and Due, 1973; Preston et al., 2003; Moffat et al.,
2011). Methadone is a chiral compound, clinically the racemic mixture of the
enantiomers is used. The two enantiomers each have different analgesic ac-
tivities, and the R(-)-isomer has the major activity (Cone and Preston, 2002)
(cited in Melis et al. (2011)). R(-)-methadone has an analgesic activity 50
times the activity of S(+)-methadone (Scott et al., 1948) (cited in Foster et al.
(2000)).

The physciochemical properties of methadone and its metabolite 2-
ethylidene-3,3-diphenylpyrrolidine (EDDP) presented in Table 8 suggest
that both compounds can partition onto particulate matter. Especially EDDP
shows a high potential for this. Their pKas suggest that they are found both
==PAGE:45==
2.2 physicochemical, pharmacological, and pharmacokinetic data
25
as neutral and ionised compounds (both compounds have base properties)
at environmentally relevant pH.
2.2.8.2
Pharmacokinetics
Methadone is mainly metabolised by cytochrome P450 isoenzyme CYP3A4
and CYP2BR in the liver and intestine (Preston et al., 2003; Moffat et al.,
2011). Other isoenzymes have also been showed to mediate the transfor-
mation, albeit with a low activity (Leimanis et al., 2012). Both methadone
enantiomers are transformed to EDDP by N-demethylation. EDDP is the
main metabolite and it is not an active metabolite. EDDP is in turn further
N-demethylated to 2-ethyl-5-methyl-3,3,-diphenylpyraline (EMDP), which
is also inactive. Furthermore, hydroxylation to methadol and subsequent N-
demethylation to normethadol has also been shown to occur (Moffat et al.,
2011). Moreover, the metabolism of methadone has been shown to be stere-
oselective, where the R(-)-methadone was shown to exhibit a lower clearance
rate than S(+)-methadone (Foster et al., 2000). Nine metabolites in human
urine have been identiﬁed (Sullivan and Due, 1973). The metabolic pathway
is shown in Figure 7.
For patients in methadone treatment, approximately 20%-60% of a dose is
excreted within 24 hours in the urine. Of this approximately 33% is excreted
as methadone, approx. 43% as EDDP, and approx 5%-10% as EMDP (Moffat
et al., 2011). The excretion of the unchanged drug is dependent upon the
pH of the urine, where a decrease in pH results in an increase of methadone
excreted (Bernard et al., 2007). Excretion with faeces is not negligible, as up
to 30% of the dose may be excreted this way. The majority of the excretion
products are unconjugated (approximately 75%) (Moffat et al., 2011).
Figure 7: Metabolic transformation of methadone in man. Adapted from Preston et
al. (2003).
==PAGE:46==
26
literature review
2.2.8.3
Degradation pathways
Senta et al. (2014) assessed the stability of methadone and EDDP in raw
wastewater at two different temperatures (10◦C and 20◦C). The experimen-
tally obtained half-lives by ﬁtting of pseudo-ﬁrst order kinetics were greater
than 200 hours at both temperatures (Senta et al., 2014). This suggests rather
high stability, which was also concluded by Castiglioni et al. (2006), Nuijs
et al. (2012), and Baker et al. (2012). Transformation of methadone and
EDDP were observed, however, it was considered to be negligible. Gonzalez-
Marino et al. (2010) observed a 20% decrease of methadone in a sample of
sewage after one day.
Baker et al. (2012) and Subedi and Kannan (2014) showed that both
methadone and EDDP sorbed readily to the suspended particulate matter.
Subedi and Kannan (2014) found that 41.1% of methadone was sorbed to
the suspended particulate matter present in WWTP inﬂuent. For EDDP this
was found to be 38.2%. Baker et al. (2012) suggested a lower fraction parti-
tioning onto suspended matter; 8.1%-18.6% for methadone and 12.1%-34.5%
for EDDP. The critical evaluation done by Baker and Kasprzyk-Hordern
(2011) showed that methadone and EDDP are subject to transformation
when stored at ambient temperature in raw and ﬁltered wastewater (19◦C,
pH 7.4). Boleda et al. (2009) found that the EDDP concentration was higher
in WWTP efﬂuent than in the inﬂuent, possible due to the transformation
of methadone to EDDP.
2.2.9
THC
Cannabinoids originate from cannabis plants such as Cannabis sativa, C. in-
dica, C. ruderalis (Melis et al., 2011). The plants contain more than 460 differ-
ent chemicals of which 60 are termed cannabinoids (Huestis, 2007).
∆9-Tetrahydrocannabinol (THC) has been shown to be the primary hallu-
cinogenic chemical. Cannabis is used recreationally but is also used med-
ically for different conditions, e.g. increase appetite and reduce vomiting
and nausea in cancer patients (Huestis, 2007). Cannabidiol (CBD) is con-
sidered non-psychocative and is used for therapeutic applications (Huestis,
2007; Bergamaschi et al., 2013). Cannabinol (CBN) is a less potent cannabi-
noid present in the cannabis plant and is also a metabolite of THC (Huestis,
2007).
2.2.9.1
Physicochemical properties
Relevant physicochemical properties of all the investigated compounds are
listed in Table 9. The properties indicate that all compounds are very lipo-
hophilic. The pKas of all but THC-COOH suggests that they neutral at envi-
ronmentally relevant pH.

2.2.9.2
Pharmacokinetics
THC is hydroxylated to 11-hydroxy-∆9-THC-9-carboxylic acid (THC-OH) in
the liver by CYP2C9 enzyme (cytochrome P450). Following hydroxylation, it
is oxidised to 11-oxo-THC, and then oxidised further to 11-nor-9-carboxy-∆9-
THC (THC-COOH) (Moffat et al., 2011). THC is also oxidised to 8-hydroxy-
∆9-THC by CYP3A4 (Moffat et al., 2011). The metabolic pathway is shown
in Figure 8.
The excretion products are both in free and conjugated form (Moffat et al.,
2011). Up to 25% of a dose is excreted as THC-COOH glucuronide in the
urine with the other carboxylic acids (Moffat et al., 2011). In the urine, THC-
COOH is the dominating metabolite, THC-OH conjugates constitute approx.
2% of the excretion products, and THC is only present at trace levels (Melis
et al., 2011). More than 65% of a dose is excreted in faeces, where THC-OH
is the predominant form (Huestis and Cone, 1998).
CBD is primarily metabolised by CYP2C19 and CYP3A4 in the liver (Jiang et
al., 2011) (cited in Bergamaschi et al. (2013)). The phase II metabolism is me-
diated by UDP-glucuronosyltransferases resulting in the formation of less
hydrophobic metabolites (Harvey and Meschoulam, 1990) (cited in Bergam-
aschi et al. (2013)). High percentages of CBD are excreted with the faeces
(Wall et al., 1976) (cited in Bergamaschi et al. (2013)).
CBN can form from the decomposition of THC exposed to air, heat, or light
(Huestis, 2007).
Many different chemical compounds exist in cannabis, hence a multitude
of different metabolic products exist (Huestis, 2007), however, the ones tar-
geted in this study are the predominating excretion products, and have been
targeted in many other studies (e.g. Baker and Kasprzyk-Hordern (2011)
and Gonzales et al. (2013)).
==PAGE:48==
28
literature review
Figure 8: Metabolism of ∆9-Tetrahydrocannabinol (THC) in man. Adapted from
Melis et al. (2011).
2.2.9.3
Degradation pathways
Numerous studies have been conducted to assess the stability of THC. Gon-
zales et al. (2013) tested the stability of THC-COOH and found that when
stored at -20◦C, the concentration declined over time. Boleda et al. (2009)
analysed the THC and THC-COOH concentration in the inﬂuent and efﬂu-
ent of WWTPs and found THC-COOH to persist in the efﬂuents samples
(average removal of 31%), whereas THC was efﬁciently removed (97%).
Due to their lipophilic nature, THC and THC-OH are difﬁcult to detect in
the wastewater (Melis et al., 2011).
2.3
methods and results from other transformation studies
Depending upon the purpose of the transformation experiment, different
experimental approaches have been used. Either the biomarkers of interest
are spiked into the matrix and the deuterated analogues are used as internal
standards (Nuijs et al., 2012), or the deuterated biomarker is spiked into the
matrix to monitor the degradation pathways (Thai et al., 2014). Moreover,
experiments where only the concentration initially present in the wastewa-
ter sample is used also exist (Plósz et al., 2013b). Apart from these, many
experiments have evaluated the stability of the biomarkers (reviews can be
found in Baker and Kasprzyk-Hordern (2011) and Senta et al. (2014)).
In order to brieﬂy summarise studies similar to the one undertaken in this
thesis, an overview of the experimental methods and investigated matrices
of a selection of biomarker transformation experiments are presented in
Table 10. The experimental ﬁndings are tabulated in Table 11.
The experiments done by Thai et al. (2014) resemble the experiments carried
out during the thesis work the most. Like in this thesis, Thai et al. (2014)
tested the impact of redox conditions and bioﬁlms on the transformation

2.4
in-sewer processes and their modelling
The sewer has for many years and in many aspect mainly been consid-
ered to serve as the integral transport function the urban wastewater sys-
tem (sewer, wastewater treatment plant, and receiving waters) (Ashley et
al., 1999; Huisman and Gujer, 2002; Hvitved-Jacobsen et al., 2013). The last
decades has seen a transition away from this notion and instead consider the
sewer system as a bioreactor mediating many different processes (Ashley et
al., 1999; Hvitved-Jacobsen et al., 2002; Huisman and Gujer, 2002; Hvitved-
Jacobsen et al., 2013). The sewer is a complex and highly dynamic system,
and can be thought to consist of four different and interacting compart-
ments: sewer atmosphere, bulk water phase, sediments, and bioﬁlm (Huis-
man, 2001; Hvitved-Jacobsen et al., 2013). The different phases interact with
each other and exchange compounds (electron acceptors and donors) across
the phase boundaries (Hvitved-Jacobsen et al., 2002). The redox conditions
may be aerobic, anoxic, or anaerobic, e.g. in the bioﬁlm the redox condi-
tions can change with depth (Hvitved-Jacobsen et al., 2013). The reduction
of sulphate to hydrogen sulphide is a clear example of how the processes
occurring in the sewer can affect its surroundings (Hvitved-Jacobsen et al.,
2002).
==PAGE:50==

literature review

1 The reported models were either linear or quadratic. In order to estimate tranformation rates in a pseudo-ﬁrst order
model framework, the ﬁtted quadratic equations were used to construct a data set to which a ﬁrst-order kinetic (decay)
model was ﬁtted. This will of course result in highly uncertain transformation rates. Quadratic models were ﬁtted to co-
caine and 6-MAM transformation. For the linear models the determined slope is used to estimate the transformation rate
with zero order kinetics. The measured concentrations were normalised by the initial concentration, hence the unit of %

evaluating transformation of benzoylecgonine found it to be stable. n.s.: not signiﬁcantly deviating from zero
In many key aspects, the sewer system differs from the activated sludge sys-
tem commonly employed at wastewater treatment plants (Hvitved-Jacobsen
et al., 2002; Huisman, 2001; Céu and Butler, 2002). The presence of a bioﬁlm
and presence of different redox conditions interacting is one aspect, however
the microbial structure is also very different. In the sewer, the biomass is pre-
dominately heterotrophic, whereas in the treatment plant different environ-
mental pressures can be introduced to favour the growth of different micro-
bial systems (e.g. favour the growth of autotrophic bacteria to facilitate the
nitriﬁcation processes) (Tchobanoglous et al., 2013). Moreover, the hydraulic
retention time is on the order of hours, whereas it is signiﬁcantly higher
in treatment plants. Consequently, the growth of the suspended biomass in
the sewer is not considered to be substrate-limited (Hvitved-Jacobsen et al.,
2013).
Several approaches to model in-sewer processes exist (see Hvitved-Jacobsen
et al. (1998a), Huisman (2001), Huisman and Gujer (2002), and Hvitved-
Jacobsen et al. (2013)) and some of these will brieﬂy be presented in the fol-
==PAGE:51==
2.5 lundtofte treatment plant and catchment
31
lowing. Huisman (2001) and Huisman and Gujer (2002) presented a model
based on the Activated Sludge Model no. 3 (ASM3). The major difference be-
tween the ASM1 and ASM3 is the inclusion of a storage variable and growth
on the stored substrate (Henze et al., 2008). Huisman (2001) was able to
ﬁnd signiﬁcant concentrations of storage compounds present in the bioﬁlm
which is one of the reasons the ASM3 was chosen as a starting point. Huis-
man (2001) was able to quantify the presence and activity of both sulphate-
reducing bacteria (SRB) and sulphide-oxidising bacteria (SOB) in a bioﬁlm
sample taken directly from a sewer, however, he concluded that their im-
pact on the carbon transformation and oxygen uptake could be considered
negligible.
The Wastewater Aerobic/anaerobic Transformations in Sewers (WATS)
model is based on the Activated Sludge Model no. 1 (ASM1), and has been
developed and extended continuously for more than a decade (Hvitved-
Jacobsen et al., 2013). The model is designed to consider both aerobic, anoxic,
and anaerobic conditions in the sewer, as well as the inﬂuence of suspended
biomass, attached biomass (bioﬁlm), and the sewer sediments. It has a well-
documented model and experimental set-up (Hvitved-Jacobsen et al., 2013).
Moreover, it has been widely used as a starting point for the modelling of
sewer processes (e.g. Mourato et al. (2003), Abdul-Talib et al. (2005), Calabrò
et al. (2009), and Rudelle et al. (2011)).
The chosen model should reﬂect the modelling needs and the possibility
to adequately obtain the experimental data needed to apply the model in
a satisfying manner (Henze et al., 2008). Hence, due to its comprehensive
description and evaluation, the WATS model was chosen as the framework
to model the primary metabolism of the biomass present in the bioreactors.
When the proposed descriptions were found inadequate to describe the ob-
servations, WATS was supplemented with other work founded on the WATS
framework (e.g. Rudelle et al. (2011) and Rudelle et al. (2012)).
2.5
lundtofte treatment plant and catchment
The wastewater used in the experiments and for the seeding of the bioreac-
tors originate from the Lundtofte Wastewater Treatment Plant (Mølleåvær-
ket). The treatment plant is designed to handle 135,000 person equivalents
and a maximum daily load of 132,000 m3 d−1. The treatment plant employs
mechanical, biological, and chemical treatment of the received wastewater
before it is discharge to Øresund. Annually, the plant treats up to 12 mil-
lion m3 wastewater (a combination of rainwater and used drinking water (5
million m3)) (Lyngby-Taarbæk Forsyning, 2015a).
The catchment covers approximately 32 km2 and it encompasses the ma-
jority of Lyngby-Taarbæk municipality and parts of Gladsaxe, Rudersdal,
and Gentofte municipalities. The sewage network consist predominately of
combined sewers (both rainwater and wastewater are conveyed to the treat-
ment plant in the same pipe) and serves predominately households but also
industry (Lyngby-Taarbæk Forsyning, 2015b). The sewage system includes
gravity mains as well as force mains (pressurised pipes) (Lyngby-Taarbæk
Forsyning, 2015a).
==PAGE:52==
==PAGE:53==
3
M E T H O D S
In the following sections, the ASM-X and WATS model frameworks used
in this thesis are presented. Moreover, the experimental methods related
to the model frameworks are also described. Lastly, the analytical methods
employed are also introduced.
3.1
process modelling and parameter estimation
3.1.1
In-sewer processes related to primary metabolism
The Wastewater Aerobic/anaerobic Transformations in Sewers model con-
cept was ﬁrst introduced and published by Hvitved-Jacobsen et al. (1998a).
In its ﬁrst rendition, the model concept focused primarily on microbiolog-
ically mediated aerobic transformation of organic carbon. Since then the
model concept has evolved to include an increasing amount of sewer pro-
cess phenomena (Hvitved-Jacobsen et al., 2013).
The model framework allows for the modelling of
• Aerobic, heterotrophic organic matter transformations
• Anoxic, heterotrophic transformations
• Anaerobic, heterotrophic transformations
• Sulphur cycle
The laboratory-scale sewage bioreactors were either completely anaerobic or
completely aerobic. Consequently, the anoxic processes (nitrate as an elec-
tron acceptor) are not presented. Furthermore, due to experimental, analyt-
ical, and temporal constraints, the bioﬁlm and the sulphur cycle were not
modelled. Due to the same circumstances, the anaerobic conditions were
not modelled either. Thus, the model framework was used to model aerobic
conditions.
3.1.1.1
Aerobic processes
In the following, the processes accounting for the aerobic transformation of
organic carbon will be presented and discussed. The redox conditions are as-
sumed to be completely aerobic at all times, hence, a Monod-like expression
for dissolved oxygen as a substrate is not included.
33
==PAGE:54==
34
methods
transformation of organic carbon
The mathematical expression
used to describe the heterotrophic growth of the suspended biomass is
based on the concept presented in the ASM1 model by Henze et al. (2000),
where the biomass grows under substrate and dissolved oxygen limiting
conditions and is described using Monod kinetics (Hvitved-Jacobsen et al.,
2013). The growth rate, rgrw [gCOD m−3 d−1], is deﬁned as
rgrw = µH
SF + SA
KSw + (SF + SA)XHw
(3.1)
where µH [d−1] is the maximum growth rate of the heterotrophic biomass,
SF is the fermentable substrate [gCOD m−3], SA is fermentation products
[gCOD m−3], and KSw is the half-saturation constant for readily biodegrad-
able substrate [gCOD m−3]. The total readily biodegradable substrate, SS,
is the sum of fermentable substrates, SF, and the products of fermentation,
SA.
Initially, an expression for endogenous respiration existed, however, exper-
imental results yielded unrealistically high decay rate constants with re-
spect to the biomass concentration (Vollertsen and Hvitved-Jacobsen, 1998;
Hvitved-Jacobsen et al., 2013). Instead, Vollertsen and Hvitved-Jacobsen
(1998) concluded that a constant maintenance energy requirement should
be included to account for the non-growth related oxygen uptake. Moreover,
the suspended heterotrophic organisms are not considered to be substrate
limited as in the activated sludge model. Hence, the decay of biomass in
sewers is omitted as this is not of great importance (Vollertsen and Hvitved-
Jacobsen, 2002), and the concept of maintenance energy requirements is a
reasonable simpliﬁcation (Hvitved-Jacobsen et al., 1998a). Endogenous de-
cay only becomes a relevant process if the readily biodegradable substrate
is depleted and not sufﬁciently augmented by the hydrolysis of particulate
substrate (Hvitved-Jacobsen et al., 2013). Thus with ample supply of oxygen,
the maintenance energy requirements rate can be described as
rmaint = qmXHw
(3.2)
where qm is the maintenance energy requirement rate constant [d−1], and
XHw [gCOD m−3] is the concentration of suspended heterotrophic biomass
in the bulk water phase.
The particulate organic matter is in the WATS framework divided into fast
and slowly hydrolysable substrate. As opposed to the inert fractions (par-
ticulate and soluble) in the ASM framework, in the WATS framework these
are omitted as they are considered to be of negligible importance due to
the comparably lower residence time in the sewer (Hvitved-Jacobsen et al.,
2013). Instead the inert particulates and inert solubles are included in the
==PAGE:55==
3.1 process modelling and parameter estimation
35
slowly hydrolysable fraction (Vollertsen and Hvitved-Jacobsen, 2002). The
hydrolysis of the particulate organic matter is described by
rhydr = khn
XSn
XHw
KXn + XSn
XHw
XHw
(3.3)
where XSn is the concentration of the hydrolysable substrate (n denotes the
different fractions (1 = fast, and 2 = slow)) [gCOD m−3], KXn is the half-
saturation constant for hydrolysable substrate [gCOD m−3], and khn is the
hydrolysis rate constant [d−1].

3.1.2
Activated sludge modelling framework for trace xenobiotic chemicals
In order to model the co-metabolic biotransformation of the biomarkers,
the Activated Sludge Modelling framework for trace Xenobiotic chemicals
(ASM-X), which has previously been used to model the biotransformation of
xenobiotics (Plósz et al., 2012) and cocaine (Plósz et al., 2013b), was adopted.
In the ASM-X framework, four state variables exist: aqueous concentration,
CLI, concentration of conjugates, CCJ, sorbed concentration, CSL, and sorbed
and inert concentration, CSL, i. However, in this thesis, only the sorbed and
the aqueous concentrations were considered.
As has been established in Section 2.2, some of the targeted compounds
have physicochemical properties that indicate they may partition onto the
suspended particulate matter as well as the deposited particulate matter
(and bioﬁlm) (Baker et al., 2012). However, the majority of the compounds
investigated do not exhibit physicochemical properties that would entail

The ASM-X model presented in Plósz et al. (2013b) was adjusted to meet
the needs and characteristics of the experimental method employed in this
investigation.The stoichiometric matrix is shown in Table 13 and Table 14
for aerobic and anaerobic redox conditions, respectively. A term to account
for abiotic processes such as chemical hydrolysis was included. The trans-
formation processes are assumed to only occur in the aqueous phase.
As will be described in Section 3.2.2, experiments were carried out in order
to estimate the sorption capacity in the experimental systems. The processes
described by the ASM-X model were then used to estimate the solid-liquid
partitioning coefﬁcient as well as the desorption rate of the biomarkers.
