the effect of the ph
It is important to consider the effect of pH on
the protonation and deprotonation of the investigated compounds as this
can have a profound effect on the sorption capabilities (Trapp and Matthies,
1998; Franco, 2010). From the pKa of the compound a lot can be inferred
regarding its behaviour in an aqueous solution. When the pH is higher than
the pKa, the compound will be deprontonated, when the pH is lower than
the pKa, the compound will be protonated (Trapp and Matthies, 1998). The
Henderson-Hasselbalch equation can help elucidate how large a fraction is
either protonated or deprotonated at a given pH (how the compound is
speciated)
pH = pKa + log
 
deprotonated
protonated
!
(3.12)
When acids are protonated they exhibit no net charge, whereas they exhibit
a negative charge when they are deprontonated. The opposite is true for
bases, i.e. bases exhibit no net charge when they are deprotonated and a net
positive charge when they are protonated. Eq. (3.12) can be re-written as
φ =
1
1 + 10a(pH−pKa)
(3.13)
==PAGE:59==
3.2 experimental set-up and procedure
39
where φ denotes the fraction of non-ionised molecules as a function of pH
and the compound speciﬁc pKa. a = −1 for bases and a = 1 for acids (Trapp
and Matthies, 1998). For zwitterions and amphoteric compounds other sim-
ilar speciation equations exist (Franco, 2010).
When the compound is charged, the assumption of sorption solely due to
hydrophobic interactions is insufﬁcient to describe the sorption potential
as electrostatic interactions between the compound and the particle surface
may become important. The pH also affects the surface charge of the sus-
pended particles (Franco, 2010; Gulde et al., 2014), and a change in pH may
cause a change of the surface charge (Shaw, 1992). Gulde et al. (2014) showed
that the pH has an effect on the biotransformation of xenobiotic compounds
in activated sludge. The biotransformation rate was found to be positively
correlated with the neutral fraction of the micropollutant, hence, for bases
this means that the biotransformation rate would be higher at higher pH.
3.2
experimental set-up and procedure
3.2.1
Wastewater characterisation
The WATS model differs in many key aspects from the ASM model. This
is evident in the methods employed to adequately determine the state vari-
ables, kinetic, and stoichiometric parameters needed by the model. The dif-
ference also relies on the fact that the model parameters should be identiﬁed
in relation to the theoretical concept in which these model parameters are
to be used (Vollertsen and Hvitved-Jacobsen, 1999).
The model parameters can explicitly be determined from well deﬁned ex-
periments by measurement of the oxygen uptake rate (OUR) and analysis
of the resulting respirogram from wastewater samples to which a known
quantity of readily biodegradable substrate (SS) has been added (Vollertsen
and Hvitved-Jacobsen, 1999). Hence, the kinetic model parameters: the max-
imum speciﬁc growth rate µH, afﬁnity coefﬁcient for SS (KS), yield constant
for the heterotrophic biomass (YH), and the maintenance energy require-
ment rate (qm) can be determined experimentally (Vollertsen and Hvitved-
Jacobsen, 1999). Spanjers et al. (1999) also showed that it is possible to obtain
parameters directly from respirograms.
The maximum hydrolysis rate has also been shown to be possible to attain
experimentally, albeit in a model framework different from the one used
in here (Céu and Butler, 2002). When the stoichiometric and kinetic model
parameters are determined, it is possible to determine the different COD
fractions present initially in the sample using the attained model parameters
(Vollertsen and Hvitved-Jacobsen, 2002).
This approach is not unique to the WATS model framework but has also
been used for estimation of kinetic parameters in activated sludge mod-
elling (Kappeler and Gujer, 1992), and to characterise domestic wastewater
(Sollfrank and Gujer, 1991; Wentzel et al., 1995). The procedure relies on
well-designed experimental set-ups which allow the identiﬁcation of dis-
==PAGE:60==
40
methods
tinct and different phases in the growth and hence uptake of oxygen by
the heterotrophic biomass present in the sample (Kappeler and Gujer, 1992).
To properly assess the different COD fractions in a wastewater sample, the
individual components must be dominant at the different phases (Spanjers
et al., 1999). This permits simplifying assumptions to be made regarding
the processes described in the model (cf. Table 12) (Vollertsen and Hvitved-
Jacobsen, 1999).
In the following subsections and paragraphs, the determination of the
model parameters needed for the WATS model is described in detail based
on the available literature (Vollertsen and Hvitved-Jacobsen, 1999; Vollertsen
and Hvitved-Jacobsen, 2002; Hvitved-Jacobsen et al., 2013).
3.2.1.1
Determination of model parameters needed in the aerobic WATS model
experimental set-up and procedure
The methods suggested in
the literature are to monitor the aqueous concentration of oxygen and
change thereof (Sollfrank and Gujer, 1991; Kappeler and Gujer, 1992;
Wentzel et al., 1995; Vollertsen and Hvitved-Jacobsen, 1999; Mathieu and
Etienne, 2000; Céu and Butler, 2002; Vollertsen and Hvitved-Jacobsen, 2002).
Moreover, the experimental procedure and method is well described in lit-
erature (Hvitved-Jacobsen et al., 2013). However, the method to be used is
constrained by the availability of instruments and equipment. As a conse-
quence, in the set-up employed in this thesis, the equipment used for the
experiments are OxiTop-C measurement heads in conjunction with the Oxi-
Top OC 110 controller from WTW Wissenschaftlich-Technische Werkstätten
GmBH (cf. Figure 10).
The OxiTop system is routinely applied at wastewater treatment plants to
assess the biological oxygen demand (BOD) of the inﬂuent and efﬂuent
wastewater (Reuschenbach et al., 2003; Roppola, 2009). The instruments mea-
sure the pressure change in the head space of a closed vessel of a known
volume containing a known volume of wastewater. The method used is
Figure 10: The OxiTop system from WTW Wissenschaftlich-Technische Werkstätten
GmbH (a part of Xylem inc.). Taken from WTW GmbH (n.d.)
==PAGE:61==
3.2 experimental set-up and procedure
41
therefore manometric respirometry. This approach can be considered as a
surrogate for measurement of the aqueous oxygen concentration. The un-
derlying assumption is that the change in pressure is solely ascribed to the
diffusion of O2 across the liquid-air interface due the the use of O2 in the
aqueous phase during the transformation of organic material. The produced
CO2 is removed by a CO2 scavenger (NaOH pellets) placed in the head
space in a rubber sleeve. The NaOH is hygroscopic, hence any moisture in
the headspace will dissolve the NaOH pellets. The CO2 is assumed to be
scavenged rapidly
CO2(g) + 2NaOH(s) →Na2CO3(aq) + H2O(l)
(3.14)
The measured pressure change can be considered equal to the oxygen up-
take rate by the biomass, if the oxygen transfer rate (OTR) across the inter-
face is assumed to be equal to or higher than the oxygen uptake rate (OUR)
OTR ≥OUR
(3.15)
The pressure change is converted to a change in oxygen concentration using
the following equation (Reuschenbach et al., 2003).
BOD = M(O2)
RT

where M(O2) is the molecular weight of oxygen (32 g mol−1), R is the
ideal gas constant (83.144 mbar (mol K)−1), T20 is the incubation temper-
ature (293.15K = 20◦C), Vtotal is the total volume of the test vessel, Vliquid is
the liquid volume in the test vessel, α is the Bunsen absorption coefﬁcient
(0.03103), and ∆p(O2) is the change of the partial pressure of oxygen [mbar]
(Reuschenbach et al., 2003). Hence, the obtained data are the cumulative
oxygen uptake. This has to be transformed to oxygen uptake rate – i.e.
OUR(t) = dBOD

The experimental procedure consists of very few steps and is in general
explained in great detail in the accompanying manual (WTW GmbH, n.d.).
==PAGE:62==
42
methods
• A wastewater sample from the inlet to the Lundtofte Wastewater Treat-
ment Plant (minimum 5 L) is taken.
• A sample is also taken from the container and analysed for total COD
(CODt).
• The wastewater is aerated for 15 minutes by sparging dry compressed
air into the container to obtain full oxygen saturation.
• The desired sample volume is transferred from the container to the
needed amber measuring ﬂasks.
• The sample is diluted with tapwater either three-fold or four-fold
to decrease the headspace volume and to decrease the COD con-
tent of the sample and ensure that the oxygen transfer rate from the
headspace to the aqueous phase would not be rate limiting.
• A magnetic stirrer is placed in the measuring ﬂask.
• 1 drop of ATU/50 mL wastewater is added to inhibit nitriﬁcation.
• Two pellets of NaOH are placed in the rubber sleeve
• The OxiTop-C measurement head is securely tightened to the measur-
ing ﬂask, thus sealing the ﬂask.
• The OxiTop-C is is then controlled with OxiTop OC 110 using the BOD
special programme.
• The bottles are placed in a 20◦C incubator for the duration of the ex-
periment.
data handling
The most important assumption in the estimation of
the parameters is the assumption of abundant supply of oxygen - i.e. the
biomass is never oxygen-limited as the oxygen transfer rate is higher than
the oxygen uptake rate. To evaluate whether or not this is observed, the
respirometric data is evaluated as suggested by Smets et al. (1996) before it
is used for parameter estimation. In the article, the authors suggest to plot
the OUR against the cumulative oxygen uptake to assess whether or not the
processes are limited by the transfer of oxygen into the liquid. If the system
is not oxygen limited, the plot will show a monotonically increasing curve
until the system is substrate limited and a precipitous drop occurs (Smets
et al., 1996). If the system is oxygen limited, the graph will show a plateau
that will prevail until the precipitous drop (cf. Figure 11).
The data acquired from the experiments are also subject to data ﬁltration
due to its highly scattered nature. The scattering is believed to be due to
the measurement principle and frequency. This entails averaging the OUR
over predeﬁned time interval based on the sampling frequency and data
==PAGE:63==
3.2 experimental set-up and procedure
43
Figure 11: The plots are taken from Smets et al. (1996) and depict the impact of
oxygen transfer rate (OTR) limitations on the oxygen consumption. (A) depicts the
cumulative oxygen consumption (the BOD measured by the OxiTops), and (B) shows
the oxygen uptake rate (OUR) plotted against the cumulative oxygen consumption.
The experiment involved two different ﬂasks with different initial substrate concen-
trations (dashed line and solid line). In (B) it is easily seen that ﬂask 1 is limited by
the OTR due to the presence of a plateau.
density, e.g. 8 minutes, 12 minutes, 20 minutes, 40 minutes, and 80 minutes,
as shown in Eq. 3.18
OUR(taverage) =

(3.18)
Where taverage is the median sampling time. Hence, the average OUR is
determined based on the sampling done between e.g. tstart = 20 min and
tend = 40 min. If the sampling frequency is 4 minutes, then ﬁve mea-
surements are registered and the average OUR is then assumed to oc-
cur at taverage = 30 min. Furthermore, the ﬁrst non-zero and non-negative
OUR(taverage) obtained is used as OUR(t = 0).
Reuschenbach et al. (2003) and Roppola (2009) evaluated the precision and
accuracy of the manometric respirometric test and concluded that the Oxi-
Top exhibits a fairly good precision, with low relative standard deviations
between identical aliquots. Moreover, the replicate experiments (duplicate
or triplicate depending on the availability of equipment) were conducted to
ascertain the reliability and reproducibility of the measurements.
data interpretation
The processes related to aerobic conditions for
the suspended heterotrophic biomass subject to the assumption of ample
supply of oxygen can be seen in Table 12.
The experimental procedure outlined in Hvitved-Jacobsen et al. (2013) rec-
ommends that the central parameters, i.e. kinetic and stoichiometric param-
eters, are determined ﬁrst followed by the determination of the COD com-
ponents. This approach is followed in the following explanation of how the
different model parameters can be estimated.
==PAGE:64==
44
methods
Kinetic and stoichiometric parameters
growth yield, YHw
When a respirogram is used to fractionate and char-
acterise wastewater, the most sensitive parameter is the growth yield, YHw,
as this is the only stoichiometric coefﬁcient (cf. Table 12) and is used to con-
vert the measured oxygen uptake to substrate uptake (Sollfrank and Gujer,
1991; Vollertsen and Hvitved-Jacobsen, 1999; Vanrolleghem et al., 1999; Céu
and Butler, 2002). When the growth yield is known, all kinetic parameters
and initial concentrations can be identiﬁed (Mathieu and Etienne, 2000).
The growth yield is deﬁned as the biomass produced per substrate con-
sumed, and can mathematically be formulated as (Sollfrank and Gujer, 1991)
YH = biomass produced
substrate consumed
(3.19)
Experimentally, the growth yield can be estimated by addition of a known
amount of substrate to a batch reactor where all readily biodegradable and
fast hydrolysable substrate have been depleted. The growth yield can then
be determined by setting up a mass balance on the oxygen and substrate
consumption. The addition of substrate results in a marked increase of mea-
sured oxygen uptake (Sollfrank and Gujer, 1991). The yield can be calculated
from the net oxygen uptake rate (Sollfrank and Gujer, 1991; Vollertsen and
Hvitved-Jacobsen, 1999).
However, in the WATS framework the SS, and concomitantly SO, is also
being used by the biomass for maintenance purposes. As a consequence
it is important to distinguish between the oxygen used for other purposes
than biomass increase (Vollertsen and Hvitved-Jacobsen, 1999). Hence, the
growth yield is at all times deﬁned as the ratio between the biomass pro-
duced and the substrate used for this speciﬁc purpose
YH = −
∆XHw
∆SS, growth
(3.20)
Where is ∆XHw [gCOD m−3] is the change in biomass, and ∆SS, growth
[gCOD m−3] is the substrate consumed for growth. This also means that the
methods proposed by Sollfrank and Gujer (1991) and in the ASM1 (Henze
et al., 2000) cannot be used directly as their model description differs from
the WATS model (Vollertsen and Hvitved-Jacobsen, 1999).
Recognising that only the total oxygen consumption (∆SO) in conjunction
with the added amount of substrate (SS, added) are measured and known,
all oxygen and substrate consuming processes need be included in the mass
balance to adequately calculate the growth yield. This means that the change
in biomass can be described as (Vollertsen and Hvitved-Jacobsen, 1999)
==PAGE:65==
3.2 experimental set-up and procedure
45
∆XHw, growth + ∆SS + ∆SO = 0 ⇔
∆XHw, growth = −(∆SS + ∆SO)
∆XHw, growth = −(∆SS, growth + ∆SO, growth + ∆SS, maint + ∆SO, maint) (3.21)
Where ∆SO [gCOD m−3] is the change in oxygen concentration for either
growth or maintenance (maint). Moreover, as SS is also continuously pro-
duced by hydrolysis, a mass balance can also be set up around SS produc-
tion and consumption. All SS is assumed to be used either for maintenance
or growth, hence
SS, added + ∆SS, prod + ∆SS, growth + ∆SS, maint = 0 ⇔
∆SS, growth = −(SS, added + ∆SS, prod + ∆SS, maint)
(3.22)
Inserting Eq. (3.22) in Eq. (3.21) yields
∆XHw, growth = SS, added + ∆SS, prod −∆SO, growth −∆SO, maint
(3.23)
Where SS, added [gCOD m−3] is the exogenous substrate added, and ∆SS, prod
[gCOD m−3] is the substrate produced from hydrolysis. Inserting Eq. (3.22)
and Eq. (3.23) in Eq. (3.20) gives (Vollertsen and Hvitved-Jacobsen, 1999)
YH = −
SS, added + ∆SS, prod −∆SO, growth −∆SO, maint
−(SS, added + ∆SS, prod + ∆SS, maint)
(3.24)
The resulting equation is slightly different from the one derived by Vollert-
sen and Hvitved-Jacobsen (1999). The authors include a term for stored sub-
strate (∆SS, stored) in the mass balance described by Eq. (3.22). However, as
the model framework does not include storage of substrate, this term has
been omitted (the authors also assume this term to be zero (Vollertsen and
Hvitved-Jacobsen, 1999)).
As previously noted, it is only possible to directly determine the amount
of substrate added and total oxygen consumed, hence the experiments are
conducted when the biomass concentration is at a steady state. This occurs
when the maintenance energy requirements are adequately met by the hy-
drolysis of hydrolysable substrate
∆SS, maint = ∆SS, prod
∆SS, maint = khn
XSn
XHw
KXn + XSn
XHw
XHw
(3.25)
If XHw ≪XSn then ∆SS, prod depends in the ﬁrst order on the biomass
concentration. The substrate produced is then wholly used for maintenance
==PAGE:66==
46
methods
throughout the duration of consumption of the added substrate. This is
explained by the notion that the increased biomass will hydrolyse the exact
amount of SS, maint needed to meet the increased maintenance requirement
- i.e. ∆SS, maint −∆SS, prod = 0. Moreover, ∆SS, maint + ∆SO, maint = 0. Hence,
Eq.(3.24) can be simpliﬁed to (Vollertsen and Hvitved-Jacobsen, 1999)
YH =
SS, added −∆SO, growth
SS, added
(3.26)
If XHw ≫XSn then ∆SS, prod is independent on biomass concentration and
instead depend in the ﬁrst order on the XSn concentration. The ∆SS, prod
will then be constant, and will not be sufﬁcient to sustain the mainte-
nance requirements of the increasing XHw during the consumption of added
substrate (Vollertsen and Hvitved-Jacobsen, 1999). Instead the added sub-
strate is utilised for maintenance as well, hence ∆SS, maint + ∆SS, prod +
∆SS, maint, added = 0. Where ∆SS, maint, added [gCOD m−3] is the maintenance
energy requirements due to the growing biomass.
Moreover, ∆SS, maint + ∆SO, maint = 0. Therefore, Eq.(3.24) can be simpliﬁed
to (Vollertsen and Hvitved-Jacobsen, 1999)
YH =
SS, added −∆SS, maint, added −∆SO, growth
SS, added −∆SS, maint, added
(3.27)
As previously mentioned, in order to determine the yield, the substrate is
added at a time when the OUR is equal to the maintenance, i.e.
OUR(t) = dSO, maint
dt
Immediately following the addition of substrate, the OUR describes both
the growth and maintenance of the biomass
OUR(t) = dSO, maint
dt
+
dSO, growth
dt
(3.28)
After the exhaustion of the added substrate, the OUR again returns to only
be due to maintenance energy requirements, although now the OUR is at a
higher level. This is ascribed to the increased concentration of biomass. The
difference between the maintenance requirement energy before and after the
addition of substrate is ∆SS, maint, added. An example is provided in Figure
12 based on Vollertsen and Hvitved-Jacobsen (1999).

In Kappeler and Gujer (1992), the authors operate with endogenous respi-
ration, however, they apply the same approach to estimate the maximum
growth rate. Wentzel et al. (1995) operate with death of biomass and growth
on SS concomitantly with growth on adsorbed hydrolysables, however, a
similar expression is derived. Moreover, Céu and Butler (2002) also use a
similar expression for determination of µH. Kappeler and Gujer (1992) also
note that the maximum growth rate is a function of the temperature, reac-
tor conﬁguration, as well as culture history (Céu and Butler, 2002). The µH
is solely determined from the initial exponential growth and not from the
exponential growth due to added substrate.
substrate affinity coefficient, KSw
The afﬁnity coefﬁcient can be de-
termined from the precipitous drop that occurs when the substrate is nearly
exhausted/depleted (Kappeler and Gujer, 1992; Spanjers et al., 1999; Vollert-
sen and Hvitved-Jacobsen, 1999). Spanjers et al. (1999) present a method to
identify the afﬁnity coefﬁcient by taking the second derivative of the oxy-
gen uptake rate. In the inﬂection point the second derivative is equal to zero
(Spanjers et al., 1999).
d2OUR(t)
dt2
= 0 if SS = 1
2KSw
(3.32)
==PAGE:69==
3.2 experimental set-up and procedure
49
However, to be able to do so the data density has to be sufﬁciently high.
Spanjers et al. (1999) estimate that ﬁve to ten data points are needed to iden-
tify a component. As the drop is precipitous, others recommend to estimate
KSw by ﬁtting the model to the acquired data (Sollfrank and Gujer, 1991;
Kappeler and Gujer, 1992; Vollertsen and Hvitved-Jacobsen, 1999). The lower
the KSw, the more precipitous the drop (Kappeler and Gujer, 1992). Due to
the scatter and low density of measurements ascribed to the experimental
setup, KSw was estimated from model calibration.
maintenance requirement rate, qm
When all initial SS and XS1 are
depleted, the maintenance energy requirements can only be met by endoge-
nous decay and/or hydrolysis of slowly hydrolysable substrates (XS2) as
described by Eq. (3.25). At this point the oxygen uptake rate is equal to the
maintenance energy requirements
OUR(t) = qmXHw(t)
(3.33)
When this situation occurs, SS is spiked. The time needed before all but XS2
is depleted differs: Vollertsen and Hvitved-Jacobsen (1999) suggest 162-168
hours, whereas Céu and Butler (2002) spike after 24 hours. Of all the arti-
cles cited, only Hvitved-Jacobsen et al. (2013) operate with the maintenance
energy requirements approach. In Vollertsen and Hvitved-Jacobsen (1999)
two ways to estimate qm are suggested. The ﬁrst approach assumes the pe-
riod of non-limited growth brought about by the addition of SS to be sufﬁ-
ciently small making biomass growth negligible. The second approach takes
biomass growth into account. Consequently, the OUR at time of spike and
onset of non-limited growth can be described as (Vollertsen and Hvitved-
Jacobsen, 1999)
OUR(t + ∆t) =
 
YHw
1 −YHw
!
µHXHw(t + ∆t) + qmXHw(t + ∆t)
(3.34)
If the biomass increase is considered negligible then XHw(t + ∆t) ∼= XHw(t),
hence Eq. (3.35) can be divided with Eq. (3.33)

t = 0 (Wentzel et al., 1995; Vollertsen and Hvitved-Jacobsen, 2002). Moreover,

Hence, the area under an OUR curve corresponds to the SS used (Vollertsen
and Hvitved-Jacobsen, 2002). Assuming that the substrate afﬁnity KSw is
sufﬁciently small, the transition from non-limited growth to limited growth
occurs with negligible change in biomass concentration (Vollertsen and
Hvitved-Jacobsen, 2002). When the initial SS(0) is depleted, the oxygen used
by the biomass can be ascribed solely to ∆SS, prod. From Eq. (3.41) it can be
seen that ∆SS, prod will be a constant fraction of the oxygen consumed. In Fig-
ure 13a this can be seen as b2/(a2 + b2) (Vollertsen and Hvitved-Jacobsen,
2002). This allows for the determination of OUR due to ∆SS, prod when the
SS has been depleted (assuming that this fraction is constant) (Vollertsen
and Hvitved-Jacobsen, 2002). Referring to Figure 13a this corresponds to
a1/b1 = a2/b2. In Vollertsen and Hvitved-Jacobsen (2002) a straight curve is
assumed to be a reasonable approximation used to separate the contribution
from the hydrolysable substrates (from Eq. (3.41) it can be seen that it is in
fact a exponential function (Wentzel et al., 1995)).
The area A above the straight line separating ∆SO, growth and ∆SO, produced in
Figure 13a can now be used to estimate the ∆SS(0) using the growth yield
(Kappeler and Gujer, 1992; Wentzel et al., 1995; Vanrolleghem et al., 1999;
Vollertsen and Hvitved-Jacobsen, 2002; Céu and Butler, 2002)

1 −YHw
(3.45)
Choi et al. (2005) compared the STOWA method and the respirogram
method for determining the concentration of SS in wastewater. They con-
cluded that using the respirogram to estimate SS can be considered accurate.
The authors used wastewater at the inﬂuent of the treatment plant, however,
they also seeded with endogenous activated sludge.

fast hydrolysable substrate
To distinguish between the contribution
from the slowly and the fast hydrolysable substrates, a similar approach
as presented in the previous paragraph is applied. When XS1 is depleted
it can be seen from Eq. (3.41) and Eq. (3.42) that the slope of the OUR
curve must change. At this point all the oxygen taken up is due to the
substrate generated from the slowly hydrolysable substrate or endogenous
respiration. Assuming a hydrolysis rate of XS2 to be ﬁrst order in XHw (i.e.
KX2 ≪XS2/XHw), the oxygen uptake ascribed to SS produced from hydrol-
ysis of XS2 can be assumed to be proportional to XHw throughout the exper-
iment (Vollertsen and Hvitved-Jacobsen, 2002). Referring to Figure 13b, this
means that a4/b4 = a3/b3. As for the separation of SS and XX1, a straight
line can then be used to approximate the contribution to the OUR from the
slowly hydrolysables. This is shown as area B in Figure 13b. Moreover, it is
assumed that the substrate needed for maintenance is adequately satisﬁed
by the hydrolysis of slowly hydrolysable substrate (Vollertsen and Hvitved-
Jacobsen, 2002). The fast hydrolysable substrate can then be determined as
XS1 =
∆SO, growth, B
1 −YHw
(3.46)
where ∆SO, growth corresponds to area B in Figure 13b.
slowly hydrolysable substrate
XS2 is not determinable from the
OUR measurements, instead it is determined from the following mass bal-
ance
CODtotal = XHw + SS + XS1 + XS2
(3.47)
This necessitates the measurement of the CODtotal at the beginning of the
experiment.
3.2.1.2
Characterisation of anaerobic microbial activity
The WATS model is also designed to model anaerobic transformation of
carbon, however due to experimental and temporal constraints, this was
not accomplished in this thesis. Nonetheless, the microbial activity during
anaerobic conditions was monitored. The characterisation and fractionation
were based on the state variables needed for the anaerobic WATS model
(Rudelle et al., 2011; Rudelle et al., 2012; Hvitved-Jacobsen et al., 2013).
Fractionation
In the anaerobic processes, the readily biodegradable substrate is divided
into fermentation products (SA) made up primarily by volatile fatty acids
(VFAs) and fermentable (readily biodegradable) substrate (SF) formed by
anaerobic hydrolysis of hydrolysable substrate. Under anaerobic conditions
==PAGE:74==
54
methods
sulphur species, sulphide and sulphate, also have a signiﬁcant role as elec-
tron acceptors, hence their determination will also be covered here.
fermentation products
SA is determined experimentally by chemi-
cal analysis of the wastewater. The method employed is based on liquid
chromatography (which is brieﬂy explained in Section 3.3.1), whereby the
concentration of different VFAs can be accurately determined. The attained
concentrations are then transformed to gCOD m−3 using the conversions
listed in Table 15 (Hvitved-Jacobsen et al., 2013).
Table 15: Conversion ratios used to convert VFA concentration to COD.
VFA
Molecular structure
Molecular weight [g mol−1]
Conversion ratio [gCOD g−1]
Formate
CH2O
46.03
0.36
Acetate
C2H4O2
60.05
1.08
Propionate
C3H6O2
74.08
1.53
Lactate
C3H5O3
89.07
1.08
Other fermentation products exist (e.g. ethanol), however the ones tabulated
in Table 15 have been found to be the predominant compounds (Rudelle et
al., 2011).
fermentable substrate
SF can be determined recognising that
SS = SA + SF ⇔SF = SS −SA
(3.48)
and after determination of SA and SS as previously described, SF is eas-
ily inferred. By taking samples at discrete time points and performing an
OUR experiment, the temporal changes in the substrate composition can be
determined as described in Rudelle et al. (2011), Rudelle et al. (2012), and
Hvitved-Jacobsen et al. (2013).
sulphur species
The sulphur species involved in the sulphur cycle are
sulphate (SO2+
4 ) and sulphide (HS−/H2S). Only sulphate is determined as
the equipment to determine the sulphide concentration was not available.
The sulphate concentration is determined using a Hach Lange cuvette test
kit. This is brieﬂy described in Section 3.3.1.
The HS−/H2S-system has a pKa of 7.1. At pH above 7.1, HS−is the domi-
nating species (Nazaroff and Alvarez-Cohen, 2001).
3.2.2
Laboratory scale sewage bioreactors
The experimental procedure revolves around the ability to distinguish the
contributions of the processes intended to be modelled. Hence, the exper-
iments are carried out in both an aerobic and an anaerobic system to de-
==PAGE:75==
3.2 experimental set-up and procedure
55
termine the effect of different redox conditions (and consequently different
bacterial communities). This is further divided in order to assess the impact
and contribution of the bioﬁlm, and of the suspended biomass. Again, this
is further subdivided in order to distinguish the effect of sorption and the
effect of biotransformation. In total, eight different experiments were con-
ducted. These eight different experiments are in turn coupled with a similar
blank experiment. Moreover, a desorption experiment is carried out with
the suspended biomass/solids used in the anaerobic sorption experiment.
Furthermore, these experiments are supplemented with another sorption
experiment in which no sparging occurred and the pH was controlled man-
ually with acetic acid. In total, 17 experiments were carried out. All the
experiments are tabulated in Table 17. Here the matrix type, redox condi-
tions, duration, matrix volume, and number of samples for each experiment
are listed.
The experimental set-up consists of two different reactor conﬁgurations:
• 2 Jacketed reactors.
• 2 Annular rotating bioﬁlm reactors (ARBRs) each connected to a jack-
eted reactor.
jacketed reactors
The jacketed reactors allow for connection with a
recirculating bath thus enabling control of the temperature. They are also
used to perform batch experiments to assess the biotransformation and sorp-
tion potential of the suspended solids/biomass, and as wastewater storage
tanks supplying the ARBRs with feed water for establishment of bioﬁlm
and its subsequent growth and maintenance. Lastly, they were used as feed
tanks in the bioﬁlm experiments. The reactors are made of polyethylene
and have a volume of 15 L. Moreover, one is able to equip the reactor with
diffusers as well as agitators (such as submerged impellers).
annular rotating biofilm reactor
The annular rotating bioﬁlm
reactors consist of an inner and an outer cylinder. The outer cylinder is sta-
tionary whereas the inner cylinder is able to rotate. The outer cylinder has
four removable slides (coupons). The coupons can be used to observe and
analyse the attached bioﬁlm. These reactors are used in the bioﬁlm exper-
iments. The reactor has four inlets and four outlets. These are positioned
on the bottom and the top of the reactor. Six of the in/outlets are sealed
off, hence only one inlet and outlet are functional. The reactor schematics
can be seen in Figure 14. The data for the ARBR can be found in Table 16.
It should be noted that the area/volume ratio in the ARBR is higher than
what is found in a real sewer, and it is also higher than what has previously
been used to assess the stability of drug biomarkers (Thai et al., 2014). This
may have implications for the resulting biotransformation rates.
description of matrices
Five different types of matrices were em-
ployed in the experiments. The wastewater was collected at Lundtofte
Wastewater Treatment Plant (Mølleåværket) close to DTU. Matrix 1 was
used for the experiments investigating the biotransformation by the sus-

pended biomass, matrix 2 was used to assess the sorption onto suspended
particles, matrix 3 was used for the biotransformation by the bioﬁlm, matrix
4 was used to determine the sorption onto the bioﬁlm, and lastly matrix 5
was used for the blank experiments.
• Matrix 1: Raw sewage inﬂuent was collected from the treatment plant
and transferred to a mixing reactor from which the volumes needed
for the experiments were drawn. The TSS concentration was approxi-
mately 320-400 mg L−1.
• Matrix 2: Primary sludge was collected and left overnight in tap wa-
ter in order to wash off compounds already sorbed to the suspended
solids. Afterwards the mixture was centrifuged for 20 minutes at 4700
RPM and the supernatant was discarded. Efﬂuent wastewater was
then collected and vacuum ﬁltered with a glass ﬁber ﬁlter with nom-
inal pore size of 0.6 µm (Advantec MFS, Inc., GA-55 grade). The two
mixtures were mixed and the biological activity was inactivated using
sodium azide (ﬁnal concentration of 0.05%). The ﬁnal TSS concentra-
tion was approximately 300-400 mg L−1.
==PAGE:77==
3.2 experimental set-up and procedure
57
• Matrix 3: Pre-clariﬁed wastewater was collected and centrifuged for
20 minutes at 4700 RPM. The supernatant was subsequently ﬁltered
by vacuum ﬁltration by use of a glass ﬁber ﬁlter with nominal pore
size of 0.6 µm (Advantec MFS, Inc., GA-55 grade). This procedure was
employed to eliminate (or greatly reduce) the suspended biomass. A
TSS concentration of approximately 0-100 mg L−1 was thus attained.
• Matrix 4: To remove sorbed biomarkers from the bioﬁlm after the bio-
transformation experiments, the ARBRs were washed with tap water
over night (12 hours). Hereafter, two coupons from each ARBR were re-
moved and suspended in 2 L tap water for 4 hours while mixing (two
different solutions, one for each bioﬁlm). The solutions were then cen-
trifuged for 30 minutes at 4700 RPM. The supernatant was discarded,
and the particles were suspended in 4 L tap water. Sodium azide was
added to inactivate the biomass (ﬁnal concentration of 0.05%).
• Matrix 5: Commercially available mineral water used for the blank
experiments. This was done to assess sorption to the reactor walls but
also to assess abiotic transformation processes. Moreover, it was also
done to assess any artefacts related to the handling and analysis.
3.2.3
Experimental procedure
biofilm
To assess the biotransformation and sorption induced by the
presence of a sewer bioﬁlm, batch experiments were performed using the
ARBRs. The growth and formation of the bioﬁlm was developed by seeding
of pre-clariﬁed wastewater in a continuous ﬂow for 12 months under ei-
ther anaerobic or aerobic conditions. Pre-clariﬁed wastewater instead of raw
wastewater was used in order to minimise possible damage of the growing
bioﬁlm due to abrasion by the larger particles present in the raw wastewa-
ter. During seeding, the ﬂow was set to 4 L d−1 and the rotating speed was
set to 20 RPM. Moreover, the reactors were covered to minimise photolytic
processes (and photosynthesis). The tubes were cleaned once a week, and
the jacketed feed reactors were cleaned every two weeks to remove any un-
wanted bioﬁlm. The reactor conﬁguration used for the bioﬁlm experiments
is shown in Figure 15. The feed water was continuously circulated between
the ARBR and the jacketed feed reactor. The jacketed feed reactor was set to
4◦C to reduce the biological activity in the feed reactor.
suspended biomass and solids
To assess the biotransformation and
sorption capabilities of the suspended biomass and solids present in the
raw sewage, two batch experiments were performed in parallel using 15 L
jacketed reactors under different redox conditions. Furthermore, a sorption
experiment with pH control as well as a desorption experiment were also
carried out.
The experimental procedures for all experiments were relatively similar, and
is described in the following:
• The thermostat for the jacketed reactors was set to 15◦C.

a For an explanation of the different matrices, refer to the descriptions in the main text.
==PAGE:79==
3.2 experimental set-up and procedure
59
• The pH, oxidation-reduction potential (ORP), and temperature were
continuously recorded for all experiments. For the biotransformation
experiments, the dissolved oxygen (DO) concentration was also mea-
sured continuously.
• The initial concentration of biomarkers in the experiments was set to
be 10 µg L−1. The stock solution concentration was 41.67 µg L−1, hence
the spiked volume depended on the reactor volume used in the exper-
iment.
• Samples were taken before the spiking of biomarkers to quantify the
background concentration.
• After spiking of biomarkers, the mixing velocity was slowly increased
to 100 RPM to assure complete mixing of the spiking solution.
• After three minutes of mixing, the mixing velocity was reduced to 60
RPM, and the t = 0 samples was taken.
• For the bioﬁlm experiments, the ﬁlling of the empty ARBR began after
three minutes of mixing.
• Hereafter, the the feed to the aeration disks was opened. For the aer-
obic reactor, atmospheric air was sparged to attain complete oxygen
saturation (DO > 9 mg L−1). In the anaerobic reactor, nitrogen was
sparged in order to rapidly induce an anaerobic environment.
• The experimental duration was 48 hours for the biotransformation
experiments, and four hours for the sorption and desorption experi-
ments.
• For the pH controlled sorption experiment without the sparging of
gas, acetic acid was manually added to maintain the pH at 7.75 ± 0.1.
• Samples for biomarkers (duplicates of 120 mL each) were taken during
the experiment together with samples for TSS, sCOD, CODt, SO2−
4 ,
NH+
4 , and NO−
3 .
• Upon sampling for biomarkers, internal standards (60 µL deuterated
analogues, 720 µg L−1, obtaining a ﬁnal concentration of 360 ng L−1)
were added, and the samples were immediately frozen in liquid nitro-
gen and subsequently stored at -20◦C. However, for all sorption and
desorption experiments, the samples were ﬁltered immediately after
addition of internal standard (vacuum ﬁltration by use of a glass ﬁber
ﬁlter with nominal pore size of 0.6 µm (Advantec MFS, Inc., GA-55
grade)) and before freezing to reduce the solid-liquid contact time.
However, the samples taken from the suspended biomass/solids sorp-
tion experiment were not frozen as they were subjected to solid-phase
extraction (SPE) immediately upon ﬁltration. For a description of the
SPE procedure, refer to Section 3.3.2.1.
• At the conclusion of the experiment, the remaining water volume was
measured to assess and quantify losses due to spillage and evapo-

water and left overnight to have the compounds desorp from the walls.
The following day, the jacketed reactors were washed.
For the desorption experiment, the volume remaining from the anaerobic
sorption experiment was centrifuged (4700 RPM for 30 minutes), a sample
of the supernatant was taken and the remainder discarded. The thickened
mixture was stored at 4◦C until the experiment was conducted the following
day. The volume of thickened solids was measured and a volume of tap wa-
ter was prepared in order to attain a ﬁnal volume of 4 L. Sodium azide was
added to the tap water to attain a ﬁnal concentration of 0.05%. The thick-
ened solids were added to the tap water and mixed (this was considered to
be t = 0). Samples for biomarkers were taken at discrete time points.
3.3
analytical methods
3.3.1
Primary metabolism
To quantify the transformation of carbon, ammonium, nitrate, and sulphur,
cuvette test kits were procured from Hach Lange and Merck Millipore
and used for the chemical analyses. Moreover, to analyse for VFAs high-
performance liquid chromatography (HPLC) was used. These are presented
brieﬂy in the following.

3.3.1.2
HPLC
Before VFA analysis, the samples were ﬁltered using 0.45 µm pore size cel-
lulose acetate ﬁlters (Minisart high ﬂow Syringe Filters from Sartorius), and
stored at -20◦C until analysis. The ﬁlters were analysed for leakage of ac-
etate as this would obscure the analysis, however, no leakage was detected.
Upon thawing, 1 mL of each sample was transferred to HPLC vials, capped
and sealed, and loaded into the HPLC. The HPLC system consists of an
Agilent Technologies 1260 Inﬁnity LC System and 1100 Series system. It
contains a binary bump (G1312A), autosampler (G1313A), variable wave-
length detector (G1314A), RI-detector (RID 1362A), a guard column system
consisting of a Micro Guard Cation-H Reﬁll Cartridge (30 mm x 4.6 mm, cat.
nr. 1250129), a Standard Cartridge Holder (cat. nr. 1250131), and a column
heater. A 6-point linear calibration curve done in duplicate was used.
The HPLC Fast Acid Analysis Column (100 mm x 7.8 mm, cat. no 1250100)
from BIO-LAB was used. The eluate was an isocratic 10 mM H2SO4 solution
at pH 3, and the employed ﬂow rate was 0.600 mL min−1. The RI temper-
ature was set to 55◦C, and the column heater set to 63◦C. The injection
volume was 40 µL, and a wave length of 210 nm was used (UV). Only data
from the variable wavelength detector was collected. The chromatograms
were analysed using the standard GC Real Time Analysis and GC PostRun
from GC Solutions.
==PAGE:82==
62
methods
3.3.1.3
Total suspended solids and volatile suspended solids
The total suspended solid (TSS) and volatile suspended solid (VSS) content
of the aliquots were determined according to the method described in ESS
Method 340.2: Total Suspended Solids, Mass Balance (Dried at 103-105◦C) Volatile
Suspended Solids (Ignited at 550◦C) (1993).
3.3.2
Drug biomarkers
The drug biomarkers and deuterated analogues were acquired from
Sigma-Aldrich. Cocaine, ecgonine methyl ester, cocaethylene, heroin, and
6-monoacetylmorphine were dissolved in acetonitrile whereas the other
biomarkers were dissolved in methanol. The analytes were acquired at an
initial concentration of 1000 mg L−1, and the deuterated analogues were
acquired at an initial concentration of 100 mg L−1.
Two different stock solutions were prepared and used. A spiking solution
containing non-deuterated compounds, and an internal standard containing
the deuterated compounds. The deuterated internal standards were added
to the samples at the time of sampling and was used to correct for matrix
effects and quantify the possible losses during sample treatment.
The stock solutions were prepared using analytical grade methanol. The
ﬁnal concentration of each analyte in the spiking solution was 41.67 µg L−1.
The ﬁnal concentration of each analyte for the internal standard solution
was 720 µg L−1. The stock solutions were stored in amber glasses in the
freezer at -20◦C until use. When the stock solutions were in use, they were
kept on ice.
3.3.2.1
Sample preparation
In each experiment 2 × 120 mL samples were taken in PE bottles and were
stored at -20◦C right after sampling as described in Section 3.2.2. The pro-
cedure employed for solid-phase extraction (SPE) is explained in the follow-
ing.
• The samples were taken up to thaw at room temperature (20◦C± 5◦).
• Upon thawing, the pH was adjusted to pH 7.0-7.5 using HCl.
• If the sample was not ﬁltered prior to freezing, the sample was ﬁltered
using a glass ﬁbre ﬁlter with a nominal pore size of 0.6 µm (Advantec
MFS, Inc., GA-55 grade).
• 100 mL of sample was measured in a volumetric ﬂask and kept on ice
whenever possible while performing the solid-phase extraction.
==PAGE:83==
3.3 analytical methods
63
• For the SPE, Oasis HLB cartridges (Oasis HLB 6 cc Vac Cartridge, 150
mg sorbent per cartridge, 30 µm particle size, 30/pk, Waters, UK) and
a Supelco Visiprep SPE Vacuum Manifold with 24 port were used.
• The cartridges were conditioned with two cartridge volumes of MeOH
and equilibrated with two cartridge volumes of MiliQ water.
• The 100 mL aliquots were eluted through the HLB cartridge at a rate
of 1 drop per minute.
• Immediately following loading, the cartridges were washed with one
cartridge volume of MiliQ water.
• Following washing, the cartridges were left to dry directly in the man-
ifold for 60-80 minutes.
• After drying, the cartridges were stored at -20◦C until elution and
chemical analysis.
The procedure employed for the sample elution and subsequent analysis is
described in the following.
• The HLB columns were loaded with 8 mL of MeOH. The eluate was
collected in silanised glass tubes placed inside the manifold.
• The glass tubes were hereafter placed in nitrogen evaporators until 250
µL remained.
• The samples were then reconstituted with 250 µL of MiliQ water and
were again evaporated to a ﬁnal volume of 250 µL determined by
weight (gravimetrically).
• 250 µL of a solution made from 80% MiliQ water and 20% MeOH were
added to the glass tubes.
• The 500 µL were transferred to a HPLC vial which was kept at -20◦C
until further analysis. The ﬁnal concentraion in the vial is 200 times
higher than the original sample.
Following thawing, the aliquots were analysed using high performance liq-
uid chromatography (HPLC) equipped with an Xbridge BEH C18 column
(130Å, 3.5µm, 2.1 mm x 150 mm) (Waters, UK). This was coupled to a hy-
brid linear Ion trap Fourier transform Orbitrap mass spectrometer (LTQ-FT
MS) (Thermo Fisher Scientiﬁc, Bremen, Germany) in positive electrospray
ionisation mode. An 11-point calibration curve was generated for each an-
alyte. After each sample sequence consisting of approximately 20 samples,
the 11-point calibration sequence was placed. This was repeated in total
three times. The calibration curve was then determined from the average
of these three sequences (for an example, refer to Appendix B.4 where the
calibration curve for cocaine is shown). All calibration curves had R2 > 0.98.
As mobile phase MiliQ and 0.05% formic acid, and MeOH and 0.05% formic
acid were used. The chromatograms were processed using Xcalibur (version
==PAGE:84==
64
methods
2.2). The analyses were performed at KWR Watercycle Research Institute
in Nieuwegein, The Netherlands by Pedram Ramin. The applied analytical
method is presented and discussed in detail in Bijlsma et al. (2013).
3.4
model implementation, calibration, and validation
3.4.1
Primary metabolism
The method employed to explicitly determine state variables and param-
eters in the WATS model framework relies on an experimental approach
that is considerably different from the one used in this thesis (see Section
3.2.1). As a result hereof, parameters that have been shown to be explicitly
determinable have to be determined through parameter calibration based
on values found in literature. Moreover, the parameters that are not explic-
itly determinable are also estimated through parameter calibration (Rudelle
et al., 2011; Rudelle et al., 2012; Hvitved-Jacobsen et al., 2013).
Calabrò et al. (2009) performed a sensitivity analysis of a model based on
the WATS framework. Herein the authors also did a literature review of the
variations of the different parameters. Their ﬁndings are shown in Table 18
together with the ﬁndings of this thesis and the data compiled by Hvitved-
Jacobsen et al. (2013).

==PAGE:85==
3.4 model implementation, calibration, and validation
65
model outputs. XHw and XS1 were shown to have the strongest inﬂuence
on the model outputs (Calabrò et al., 2009). These ﬁndings emphasises the
importance of proper and accurate determination of these fractions in the
respirometric analyses performed on the wastewater used in the biomarker
experiments. Calabrò et al. (2009) used a Monte Carlo approach with 1,000
behavioural simulations per parameter. The parameter space for each vari-
able consisted of a uniform distribution of the typical ranges presented in
Table 18. A reference simulation was made using a set of “default” parame-
ter values and the sensitivity of each parameter was estimated relatively to
the results of this reference simulation (Calabrò et al., 2009).
optimisation algorithm
To optimise the model parameters, Fmin-
search was used. Fminsearch is an unconstrained non-linear optimisation
function in MATLAB that seeks to minimise the objective function. However,
as Fminsearch is not a global optimisation algorithm, the proposed parame-
ter set is likely to represent a local minimum instead of the global minimum.
The function is based on the Nelder-Mead simplex algorithm as described
in Lagarias et al. (1998). To constrain the parameter space Fminsearchbnd
by John D’Errico was used to minimise the objective function. The function
is used completely like Fminsearch, however, the script imposes bounds on
the parameter space available to the optimisation algorithm. The objective
function was chosen to be the root mean square error (RMSE) between the
measured OUR and simulated OUR
RMSE =

(3.49)
500 iterations were performed in order to have fast computational speed
and the possibility to do the optimisation locally. The parameter constraints
can be found in Table 18.

As previously noted, the biomarkers are to some extent expected to parti-

be set up describing this partitioning and transformation of an arbitrary
compound (Ternes and Joss, 2006)

Where Cw,b is the aqueous concentration [g m−3], Cs,b is the sorbed concen-

the reactor conﬁguration can be described as a completely mixed ﬂow reac-
tor (CMFR) (Nazaroff and Alvarez-Cohen, 2001; Trapp and Matthies, 1998).
Consequently, the concentration in a sample taken from the reactor is iden-
tical to the concentration in the reactor. Moreover, no ﬂow enters or exits the
reactor (except of course when a sample is taken). Even though samples are
taken from the reactor at discrete time points, nothing is removed during
operation, and Vw may be remove from the differential term (the assump-
tion is that ∆Vw ≪Vw) (Nazaroff and Alvarez-Cohen, 2001). Eq. (3.50) can
be reduced to (essentially a batch reactor)

The adapted ASM-X model describing the biotic and abiotic transformation
and sorption of the drug biomarkers is presented as a “Gujer-type” stoichio-
metric matrix in Table 13. As shown in Section 2.2, the metabolic pathways
of the biomarkers may have an effect on the concentration of a speciﬁc com-
pound - i.e. a spiked compound may be transformed to another spiked com-
pound (e.g. methamphetamine can transform to amphetamine and other
metabolites). Hence, the ordinary differential equations can be considered a
cascade (assuming (pseudo-)ﬁrst order kinetics) (Trapp and Matthies, 1998).
As an example, the biotic and abiotic transformation of methamphetamine
and amphetamine is shown

==PAGE:87==
3.4 model implementation, calibration, and validation
67
For the bioﬁlm experiment, a CMFR can also be assumed, however, as the
system consists of two compartments (a feed reactor and the ARBR), it will
also be modelled as two different compartments in which the biotransfor-
mation is assumed to only occur in the annular rotating bioﬁlm reactor. The
two reactors are each assumed to be fully mixed, however, the system as
such is not expected to be fully mixed. Moreover, the ﬂow between the re-
actors is continuous, hence, the withdrawal of samples reduces the volume
in the feed reactor thus decreasing the hydraulic residence time in the feed
reactor, while increasing the relative hydraulic residence time in the ARBR
(increasing the contact time). The volume of the ARBR remains constant as
it is completely ﬁlled within 15 minutes from the start of the experiment.
An illustration of the system is shown in Figure 16.

optimisation algorithm
In order to obtain the best ﬁt of the transfor-
mation rates, the reaction constants are estimated by calibration of the model
to the experimental data. The method is based on the approach presented
in Sin et al. (2007). The calibration is based on the Monte Carlo method and
uses 10,000-1,000,000 iterations. The sampling space is generated using Latin
hypercube sampling (LHS) assuming a uniform parameter distribution. If
the imposed parameter space bounds are reached, the parameter space is
expanded and the optimisation algorithm is executed once again. If the sug-
gested optimal parameter set is found to be far from the imposed bounds,
the bounds are shrunken and the optimisation algorithm is executed once
again.
The objective function to be minimised is the sum of RMSE normalised to
the measured concentration for all parameters. Hence, for one parameter
RMSE(j) =

==PAGE:89==
4
R E S U LT S A N D D I S C U S S I O N
4.1
primary metabolism
During the aerobic and anaerobic biotransformation experiments, the
biomass is active. In total, four biotransformation experiments have been
conducted: one for each redox condition and for each type of biomass:
• Suspended biomass, aerobic conditions
• Suspended biomass, anaerobic conditions
• Bioﬁlm, aerobic conditions
• Bioﬁlm, anaerobic conditions
In the following sections, the experimental data pertaining to the primary
metabolism will be presented and discussed. The OUR experiments were
done in duplicates as the method applicability had not been assessed using a
respirometer prior to the biotranformation experiments, and the experience
gained from the initial scoping made it clear that the experiment should be
done in replicates. The availability of OxiTops made it possible to only do
so in duplicate.
4.1.1
Initial scoping and estimation of kinetic and stoichiometric parameters
Prior to the experiment with the biomarkers, initial scoping experiments
were performed to get familiarised with the experimental procedure as well
as to estimate a “typical” maintenance requirements rate constant and the
growth yield of the biomass present in the inﬂuent. As previously noted, this
would entail the addition of a known amount of substrate to the biomass
when the oxygen uptake is solely for maintenance needs. Elsewhere ac-
etate has been used (Vollertsen and Hvitved-Jacobsen, 1999; Sakrabani et al.,
2009), however in these experiments, propionate was used instead as this
was readily available. 50 gCOD m−3 sodium propionate was added after 40
hours. The resulting OUR proﬁle can be seen in Figure 17.
From the ﬁgure, the oxygen used for maintenance can be found from area
(3) and (2) (∆SO, maintenance + ∆SO, maintenance, added), and the oxygen used for
growth can be found from area (1) (∆SO, growth).
Using Eq. (3.26) to estimate an upper growth yield

Using Eq. (3.35) and Eq. (3.38) to estimate qm yielded a qm higher than 12
d−1. Based on the literature values presented in Table 18, this estimation
seems rather unrealistic. Instead, the pragmatic solution of qm ≈17%µHw
will be used for the modelling and fractionation purposes (Vollertsen and
Hvitved-Jacobsen, 1999; Vollertsen and Hvitved-Jacobsen, 2002). However,
the qm has been shown to vary with time, even for the “same” wastewater,
and may also vary depending on the substrate concentration (Sakrabani et
al., 2009). The same has been found to be relevant for the yield (Tempest
and Neijssel, 1984).

Scoping experiments to assess the feasibility of an alternative approach to
obtain kinetic parameters for the anaerobic model were also carried out. The
experimental set-up consisted of a 5 L glass bottle positioned on a magnetic
stirrer and closed with a cap with sampling ports protruding the cap. The
sampling ports were closed with sealing clips. Samples taken from glass
bottled were analysed for VFAs. The results are shown in Figure 18.

Unfortunately, the coupling to an OUR experiment at discrete time inter-
vals was not possible as the equipment was unavailable, hence, the temporal
variation of SS and SF was not assessed. Moreover, as this experiment only
served to ascertain whether or not anaerobic conditions could be attained,
no other analytical methods were coupled to the experiment. However, the
results presented in Figure 18 show that the suspended biomass in the raw
wastewater has a potential for acidogenesis and acetate fermentation as the
VFA and acetate concentration increases. The formation of acetate can be
due to the presence of sulphate-reducing bacteria (SRB) or acetogenic bac-
teria (Gibson, 1990; Hvitved-Jacobsen et al., 2013). If SRB were partaking in
the fermentation/anaerobic degradation, the formation of acetate would be
accompanied by a decrease in sulphate and increase in hydrogen sulphide.
When removing the cap, the pungent odour was evident of hydrogen sul-
phide production.

persist for approximately six hours. A linear regression yielded maximum
growth rates of 6.8 d−1-7.0 d−1. The estimated values are comparable to
what can be found in literature (Bjerre et al., 1998; Vollertsen and Hvitved-
Jacobsen, 1999; Vollertsen and Hvitved-Jacobsen, 2002; Sakrabani et al., 2009;
Hvitved-Jacobsen et al., 2013), albeit they can be considered in the high end.
4.1.2.2
Fractionation of inﬂuent used in experiments
The same inﬂuent is used for both the aerobic and anaerobic experiment,
hence, the fractionation of COD is relevant for both experiments, whereas
the kinetic aerobic parameters obtained are mainly relevant for the aerobic
experiment. The resulting OUR curves from the fractionation experiments
are shown in Figure 20.
For both replicates the maximum OUR is approximately 23-24 gO2 m−3
hr−1 and occurs a little more than ﬁve hours into the experiment. The ex-
haustion of readily biodegradable substrate should result in a precipitous
drop as described in Section 3.2.1, however, the temporal resolution (20 min
averages) obtained with the employed methodology does not allow for a
discernible rapid transition. If the temporal resolution is decreased, the re-
sulting plot is impractical to analyse. The depletion of SS entails a transition
to limited growth dependent on the hydrolysis rate of XS1 (kh1). This is not
easily identiﬁable in Figure 20, nonetheless the transition is assumed to oc-
cur after 8-9 hours. When all XS1 has been consumed, the oxygen uptake
is limited by the hydrolysis of XS2. The transition between XS1 and XS2 is
easier to discern than the transition from SS to XS1, and is assumed to occur
after approximately 14 hours.
The estimation of the different substrate fractions are tabulated in Table 19.
The dominating fraction was found to be the slowly hydrolysables (62%).

wastewater used for the suspended biomass experiments. The experiment lasted
24 hours, hence the pressure change was recorded every fourth minute. The data
points here represent the rate of values averaged over 20 minutes. The area above

remaining 2% are found to be heterotrophic biomass. These ﬁndings are
not congruent with the typical fractions suggested by Hvitved-Jacobsen et
al. (2013), however, the wastewater composition is highly variable, and the
experimental set-up employed in this thesis is, as previously discussed, not
identical to the approach suggested. Furthermore, the soluble COD consti-
tutes 49% of the total COD.

Figure 21: Aerobic suspended biomass biomarker transformation experiment. Mea-
surements at discrete time points throughout the experiment of (a) sulphate, (b) lac-
tate, propionate, formate, and acetate (c) nitrate and ammonium, and (d) continuous
on-line measurements of the oxidation-reduction potential (ORP).
From the ﬁgure it can be seen that the sulphate concentration increases
slightly from 52 g m−3 to 56 g m−3, whereas the nitrate and the ammonium
concentrations decrease from 0.51 gN m−3 to 0.47 gN m−3 and 64.6 gN
m−3 to 36.7 gN m−3, respectively. The concentration of nitrate can however
be considered constant, as the measured change is within the uncertain-
ties related to the analytical method itself. The VFAs are almost completely
consumed after 12 hours, and are not detectable at the end of the experi-
ment. The decrease observed for ammonium could be indicative of biomass
growth as nitrogen is used to build new cell mass. It could also be indicative
of nitrifying (autotrophic) bacteria, however, no concomitant accumulation
of nitrate is observed. Nitrite was not measured. The ORP, although it is
not constant, is indicative of aerobic conditions and not anoxic conditions.
However, if ﬂocs are formed, anoxic environments may be present in these
(Tchobanoglous et al., 2013). Moreover, the pH also increases 0.5 unit within
four hours of the experiment, and the average pH was 8.80 ± 0.06. The in-
crease in pH entails a conversion of ammonium to ammonia, which is able
to volatilise and thus be removed from the aqueous phase (Hvitved-Jacobsen
et al., 2013).
The result of the OUR analyses is used to ﬁt the aerobic WATS model to
the obtained data. The result is depicted in Figure 22(b),(d) and the kinetic
parameters obtained by curve ﬁtting are listed in Table 20. The calibrated
parameters are not at the boundaries of their predeﬁned range, and only
KSw and kh2 are similar for both of the replicates. kh1 and KX2 are the pa-
rameters that are signiﬁcantly different in the two replicates. Hence, the

Figure 22: (a) and (c) Oxygen uptake rate (OUR) curve obtained experimentally
(black empty circles) for raw wastewater. The ﬁtted model is shown in blue dashes.
The experiment was done in duplicate, and each replicate is shown. (b) and (d) Show
the resulting model based on the processes presented in Table 12.
difference in model ﬁt is mainly ascribed to the different initial conditions
in the state variables. A better ﬁt is likely to be obtained if the parameter
ranges are increased or another optimisation method is employed, however,
based on the crudeness and uncertainty of the experimental method, this is
considered superﬂuous. Moreover, the qm may be considered too high hence
resulting in a too fast endogenous respiration and decrease in biomass.
The hydrolysis rate of the fast hydrolysables is determined to be 7.16 d−1
and 2.93 d−1, which is comparable to the typical value (5 d−1) suggested
in Hvitved-Jacobsen et al. (2013), albeit higher and lower, respectively. The
kh2 was found to be higher than the typical values suggested (0.5 d−1). This
may further indicate that the chosen qm is too high.
The two models suggest a maximum concentration of heterotrophic bacteria
of approximately 120 gCOD m−3. The maximum is attained at the point
of SS depletion, i.e. after 8-9 hours. Hereafter, the biomass concentration
decreases and is projected to attain a concentration of 20 gCOD m−3 by the
end of the laboratory-scale experiment.

Figure 24: (a) Oxygen uptake rate (OUR) curve obtained experimentally (black
empty circles) for raw wastewater after 48 hours of suspended biomass aerobic ex-
periment. The ﬁtted model is shown in blue dashes. (b) show the resulting model

These results show that there is still biological activity after 48 hours. The
most interesting observation is the XHw(t = 0) estimated. The model based
on the initial fractionation predicts a ﬁnal XHw = 20 gCOD m−3, whereas
the fractionation of the 48 hour sample suggest an initial XHw(t = 0) =
10 −15 gCOD m−3. However, this may also just be coincidental. The max-
imum OUR is attained approximately 20 hours into the experiment. This
can be indicative of a low concentration of readily biodegradable substrate
or low concentration of bacteria able to utilise the available substrate or
hydrolyse the slowly hydrolysable substrate. The maximum OUR is approx-
imately half the value as the maximum obtained in the initial fractionation
(12 gO2 m−3 hr−1). From the OUR curves an initial peak can also be ob-
served, however, no likely explanation can be given for this observation.
Hydrolysis is primarily mediated by extracellular enzymes (Hvitved-
Jacobsen et al., 2013), and the late OUR peak can also be explained by a
non-acclimated biomass with negligible growth in combination with a rela-
tive high hydrolysis rate. However, these suggestions are merely speculative
and not able to be conﬁrmed nor be used to conﬁrm the model predictions.
==PAGE:98==
78
results and discussion
It is important to note that the biotransformation experiments were con-
ducted at 15◦C, whereas the OUR experiments are done in a 20◦C incubator.
Hvitved-Jacobsen et al. (2013) suggest a temperature correction factor of
1.07 for aerobic heterotrophic activities in the bulk phase. This would mean
a correction factor of 1.07(15−20) = 0.72, when transferring the ﬁndings from
the OUR experiment to the biotransformation experiment.
4.1.2.4
Anaerobic conditions
As oxygen is absent as an electron acceptor, fermentation of carbon species
as well as oxidation of these are expected to be coupled with the reduction
of sulphate (or other electron acceptors apart from oxygen). Hence, the pro-
duction of volatile fatty acids and depletion of carbon species and sulphate
were analysed throughout the duration of the experiments.
In Figure 25, the concentration changes of VFAs, nitrate and ammonium,
sulphate, and the oxidation-reduction potential are shown. From the ﬁgure,
it is evident that the concentrations of sulphate, VFAs, ammonium, and
nitrate decrease over the course of the experiment.

Figure 25: Anaerobic suspended biomass biomarker transformation experiment.
Measurements at discrete time points throughout the experiment of (a) sulphate,
(b) Lactate, propionate, formate, and acetate (c) nitrate and ammonium, and (d) con-
tinuous on-line measurements of the oxidation-reduction potential (ORP).
The sulphate concentration is for the ﬁrst 24 hours of the experiment ap-
proximately 55 g m−3. At the conclusion of the experiment, it has decreased
to 44 g m−3 (a 20% decrease).
==PAGE:99==
4.1 primary metabolism
79
The sum of VFAs is initially 120 gCOD m−3. During the ﬁrst three hours,
the acetate and propionate concentration increases slightly, and lactate and
formate are in turn depleted. After six hours, the sum of the four substrates
has decreased 35%. Between t = 6hr and t = 12hr no change in the sum can
be observed, however the propionate concentration decreases with a con-
comitant increase in acetate. After 24 hours, the total concentration has de-
creased 86%. After 48 hours the total concentration is approximately equal
to the concentration at t = 24hr, however acetate and propionate are now
present in the same concentrations (in gCOD m−3).
The sulphate-reducing bacteria are able to consume the VFAs by use of
sulphate as the ﬁnal electron acceptor. Hence, the depletion of VFAs by
SRB should result in a decrease of dissolved SO2−
4
and subsequent increase
in H2S. The measurements shown in Figure 25 seem to corroborate this
hypothesis. The oxidation-reduction potential measurements obtained are
also suggestive of sulphate-reducing conditions. However, the substantial
decrease in VFAs occurring between t = 12hr and t = 24hr is not coinciding
with the decrease in sulphate occurring between t = 24hr and t = 48hr. A
possible explanation of this discrepancy could be that the SRB are not ini-
tially present at signiﬁcant concentrations, hence their use of sulphate as an
electron acceptor is off-set by the generation of sulphate from the biological
oxidation of organic sulphur from the decay of the aerobic biomass using
nitrate (Kamp et al., 2006). However, this explanation is merely speculative.
Moreover, the fermentation products measured do not form an exhaustive
list, and the increase of ethanol, H2 or others may be a possibility. Rudelle
et al. (2011) found that ethanol did not accumulate in mature wastewater,
likely due to the presence of SRB able to utilise it immediately. SRB can use
a large variety of electron donors (Gibson, 1990).
Moreover, the ORP increases to a level above zero mV approximately 26
hours into the experiment. This is however not coupled to a sudden in-
crease in dissolved oxygen (cf. Figure 26), and may be due to a faulty probe.
However, after 40 hours, the oxygen concentration steadily increases to 3.81
g m−3 (attained at t = 47.7hr). The N2 sparging is increased and anaer-
obic conditions are quickly attained and kept until the conclusion of the
experiment. As a consequence, the anaerobic system was aerobic for approx-
imately 8 hours. Fortunately, it occurred in the ﬁnal parts of the experiment.
The data derived from the last discrete sample at t = 48hr must then be
considered to not be a true reﬂection of an anaerobic system.
The nitrate present in the system decreases concomitantly with the decrease
in ammonium. This could indicate growth of biomass by uptake of ammo-
nium as a source of nitrogen. However, the pH also increases with more
than one unit over the course of the experiment. As the ammonia/ammo-
nium system (NH3/NH+
4 ) has a pKa of 9.3, this entails a NH3 fraction of 2%
at the beginning, and a 10-fold increase to 20% at the end (Hvitved-Jacobsen
et al., 2013). Ammonia is a volatile compound, hence, the observed ammo-
nium decrease is likely to partly be attributed to the stripping of ammonia.
A sulphate reduction deammoniﬁcation process has been proposed and ev-
idence for this reaction has been presented (Fdz-Polanco et al., 2001). This
reaction consumes two moles of ammonium per mole of sulphate. However,
assuming this process to occur can not sufﬁciently explain the decrease in
==PAGE:100==
80
results and discussion
the ammonium concentration. Thus, attributing it to assimilation and volatil-
isation seems plausible.
Furthermore, the hydrogen sulphide/sulphide system is also affected by
the pH increase (cf. Figure 26), as HS−practically changes from being the
dominating specie to the sole specie (80% to 98%) (Hvitved-Jacobsen et al.,
2013). If the samples contain iron, iron and hydrogen sulphide may form
iron sulphide precipitates, e.g. FeS (Nielsen et al., 2008; Hvitved-Jacobsen
et al., 2013). At higher pH, the precipitation of iron sulphide was shown by
Nielsen et al. (2008) to increase (however iron was added to the sewage). The
increase of pH in the system could result in an increased amount of HS−
precipitating. However, as neither sulphide nor iron content were analysed,
this hypothesis cannot be tested.
The production and consumption of VFAs and sulphide has an effect on
the pH. The sulphate reduction would entail an increase in pH, whereas
the fermentation decreases the pH (Hvitved-Jacobsen et al., 2013). However,
the continuous sparging of nitrogen which effectively strips the system of
other gases than N2 makes it difﬁcult to infer any reactions based on the pH
changes.

Figure 26: Anaerobic suspended biomass biomarker transformation experiment.
Continuous measurements of temperature, pH, and dissolved oxygen (DO) through-

The change of soluble and total COD is shown in Figure 27. The impact of
the methanol is easily seen by comparing the measurements before and after
the spiking of biomarkers. Before spiking, the sCOD is 43% of the total COD.
After spiking this increases to 54%. At the end of the experiment the sCOD
is 33% of the total COD. The observed decrease is rather linear as opposed to
the observed decrease for the aerobic experiments, which readily resembles
an exponential decay. The sparging rate of the nitrogen is not as high as for
the atmospheric air in the aerobic system, and this may have implications on
the rate of evaporation of methanol. The methanol may very well be utilised
by the anaerobic biomass.
Furthermore, OUR experiments were done on samples drawn from the
batch reactor after 24 hours and 48 hours (the conclusion of the biomarker
experiment) to evaluate the primary metabolism as well as anaerobic hy-
drolysis and fermentation rate. The employed method can be considered a
modiﬁed version of the approach taken by Rudelle et al. (2011). One has to

Figure 28: OUR analysis performed on a sample drawn at t = 24 hr during the
anaerobic suspended biomass biomarker transformation experiment. The full lines
delineate what was assumed to be oxygen used for different readily biodegradable
substrates (areas above lines), and what should be considered oxygen used for main-
tenance (area below lines and not contained in the resulting “triangles”).
From the ﬁgure, two distinct peaks can be seen. One explanation could
be two different substrates, either utilised by the same heterotrophic bac-
teria or different types of heterotrophic bacteria. The exponential phases
for the substrate ﬁrst utilised is approximately ﬁve hours and begins a few
hours into the experiment. The second substrate has an exponential growth
phase of more than 12 hours. The concentration of each substrate was de-
termined from the area above the full lines. The area below the full lines
is ascribed to a baseline oxygen uptake due to maintenance. At t = 24,
CODt = 774gCOD m−3 and sCOD = 362 gCOD m−3. The ﬁrst peak cor-
responds to an oxygen uptake of 38 gO2 m−3 and the second peak to an
oxygen uptake of 80 gO2 m−3. An explanation could be that the ﬁrst oxy-
gen uptake is used in the process of storage of substrate internally in the
cell. The second peak can then be ascribed to the utilisation of the stored
substrate and substrate present in the bulk phase, which is described by
==PAGE:102==
82
results and discussion
the ASM3 (Henze et al., 2008). The total SS contained in the two peaks are
determined to be 269 gCOD m−3.
48 hours
Samples were also taken at the conclusion of the experiment
and subjected to OUR experiments. The model results are shown in Figure
29 together with the initial conditions estimated. The kinetic parameters
obtained by model ﬁt are tabulated in Table 22.
Aside from an initial peak in the OUR curves, the predominate peak occurs
after 30 hours. The initial biomass was estimated to be 10-15 gCOD m−3,
and the SS was estimated to be 160-192 gCOD m−3. For both replicates
the biomass growth is projected to be considerable based on the available
substrate. The OUR seems to level off at a rate higher than the initial OUR
before the exponential growth. The higher OUR level at the end is mainly
sustained by the hydrolysis of more complex molecules. At t = 48, CODt
= 703 gCOD m−3 and sCOD= 232 gCOD m−3. From the OUR analysis
approximately 50% of the total COD in the sample can be utilised by the
bacteria when it is incubated for 48 hours.

fermentation and hydrolysis
The data obtained from the 24 hours
and 48 hours analysis is coupled with the VFA analysis to calculate the
concentration of fermentable substrate (SF). The result is depicted in Figure
30.
From the ﬁgure, it can be seen that the SS more than doubles over the course
of 24 hours. At t = 0, the SS is almost found exclusively as VFAs. After 24
hours, the composition changes completely. The SS content increases, and is
now predominately found as fermentable substrate. The SF is formed anaer-
obically by hydrolysis of more complex molecules. The increased availability
of fermentable substrate does not result in an accumulation of VFAs. On the
contrary, the concentration of VFAs is comparable at t = 24hr and t = 48hr.
However, as previously explained, the anaerobic system was in fact aerobic
during the ﬁnal seven hours of the experiment, hence, the consumption of
VFAs by aerobic heterotrophs is likely to have obscured the data.

Figure 30: Estimation of SF based on the OUR experiments done at t = 0hr, t =

Rudelle et al. (2011) experimentally assessed the anaerobic hydrolysis and
fermentation of raw wastewater of three different systems and of three dif-
ferent maturities using the same modeling framework and experimental ap-
proach. In here, the authors showed that the SS increased continuously over
the duration of their 48 hour experiments. However, their analyses showed
that the SS was predominately found as VFA at all times. This was found
to indicate that hydrolysis and not fermentation was the rate-limiting pro-
cess (Rudelle et al., 2011). In this study, the situation seems to be completely
opposite, and rather fermentation appears to be the rate-limiting process
due to the accumulation of fermentable substrate. This observation corrob-
orates with the rather constant concentration of sulphate observed the ﬁrst
24 hours. The absence of SRB activity is shown by the lack of fermentation
and sulphate reduction.
==PAGE:104==
84
results and discussion
4.1.3
Annular rotating bioﬁlm reactor
For the experiments with the ARBR, the raw inﬂuent was ﬁrst centrifuged
and subsequently ﬁltered. To evaluate the effect of the two different solid-
liquid separation methods on the suspended biomass, OUR experiments
were done on the centrifuged supernatant as well as on the ﬁltrate. This
was done in addition to the fractionation of the raw inﬂuent. It is important
to remark that the ﬁltrate was the matrix used for the experiment.
The thickness of the bioﬁlm were estimated to be 0.75 mm for the aerobic
bioﬁlm, and 1.02 mm for the anaerobic bioﬁlm (see Appendix B for calcu-
lations). The thickness was estimated from the void volume in the reactors
and the surface area of the reactor.
4.1.3.1
Fractionation of inﬂuent
The OUR measurements of the three different experiments are shown in
Figure 31. The experiments were done in duplicates. As was also discussed
for the OUR analysis done for the suspended biomass experiments, the em-
ployed methodology does not allow for easily discernible transitions. If the
temporal resolution is decreased, the resulting plot is impractical to analyse.
The proposed transitions marked by the full lines in Figure 31 are therefore
uncertain.
Despite the crude measurements and appertaining uncertainties, it is possi-
ble from the ﬁgure to infer that centrifugation does not remove any appre-
ciable amount of biomass nor readily biodegradable and fast hydrolysable
substrate. For the raw inﬂuent, the peak OUR is found to be approximately
19 gO2 m−3 hr−1 and occur four hours into the experiment. After centrifu-
gation, the peak OUR has decreased to approximately 16 gO2 m−3 hr−1 and
occurs at a later time point (approximately six hours into the experiment).
The ﬁltration leads to a further decrease of the peak OUR (12 gO2 m−3 hr−1)
as well as further postponement of its occurrence (after 10 hours).
A likely explanation is of course the removal of both substrates concomi-
tantly with the removal of biomass. Moreover, the centrifugation also entails
a sudden temperature increase due to the heat generated in the centrifuge.
This can exacerbate the time needed for the biomass to acclimate or even
lead to inactivation depending on the severity of the temperature increase.
Moreover, as both centrifugation and ﬁltration are time consuming steps,
it is not unlikely that some substrate is utilised prior to the beginning of
the experiment. The temperature increase could also increase the microbial
activity and subsequent removal of substrate.
The result of the OUR analysis and subsequent fractionation based on the
method proposed by Vollertsen and Hvitved-Jacobsen (2002) is shown in
Table 23. Unfortunately, no measurements of the total COD of the raw and
centrifuged wastewater were done, hence the slowly hydrolysable fraction
could not be determined for these. The fractionation of the raw and cen-
trifuged wastewater yield similar results, the sum of SS and XS1 differ only
slightly. The ﬁltration appears to remove 75% of the initial biomass present

4.1.3.2
Aerobic conditions
As for the experiment with the suspended biomass, the ARBR set-up was
designed to secure an ample supply of oxygen to the biomass, resulting in
a growth only limited by the availability of substrate.
The maximum growth rates were determined from the initial exponential
increase of OUR. The plots are shown in Figure 32. The kinetic parameters
are tabulated in Table 24.
In the ﬁgure, it can be seen that the exponential growth phase seemingly
continues for approximately eight hours. A linear regression yielded a max-
imum growth rate of 4.2 d−1. The estimated values are comparable to what
can be found in literature, and lower than what was found in the suspended
biomass experiment (Bjerre et al., 1998; Vollertsen and Hvitved-Jacobsen,
1999; Vollertsen and Hvitved-Jacobsen, 2002; Sakrabani et al., 2009; Hvitved-
Jacobsen et al., 2013). Moreover, the analysis of the experiments with the
raw and centrifuged wastewater resulted in higher maximum growth rates
(see Appendix A).
The ﬁtted OUR curves and the resulting model are shown in Figure 33.
The model depictions and model parameters suggest a potential for sus-
pended biomass growth throughout the duration of the biomarker biotrans-
formation experiment. However, the maximum concentration attained after
