48 hours
The result of the OUR experiment performed at the conclu-
sion of the aerobic ARBR biomarker experiment can be seen in Figure 34.
The maximum OUR is attained almost 37 hours into the 72 hour long exper-
iment. The fractionation suggests that this is ascribable to the low content
of biomass in the experiment. This is suggestive of a rather stabile bioﬁlm
present in the ARBR, i.e. the detachment rate can be considered negligible.

Moreover, it also suggests that the suspended biomass present in the feed

VSS was found to be 13.5 ± 0.5 g m−3. This is comparable to the initial TSS
measured.

The OUR analysis show that the biomass present in the feed reactor is
indeed active, however, only a small concentration of the COD is active
biomass (0.05%). However, the oxygen demand determined in the OUR ex-
periments is higher than the total COD determined (cf. Figure 35). This
discrepancy could be due to a heterogeneous mixture – e.g. some bioﬁlm or
other larger particles may have been transferred to the BOD bottle (and not
the COD cuvette). Yet, it could also show deﬁciencies in the experimental
method employed to determine the OUR and subsequent fractionation.
The CODt and sCOD were also continuously monitored. The results are
shown in Figure 35. The initial increase due to the addition of the biomark-
ers dissolved in methanol is apparent. The sCOD attains a level similar to the
initial concentration after 12 hours. The total COD is almost exclusively con-
sisting of soluble COD. Combining this with the ﬁndings from the OUR ex-
periment done at the conclusion of the experiment suggest that the majority
of the COD remaining in the system is predominately slowly hydrolysable
compounds (the hydrolysis rate is estimated to be 0.5 d−1).
In Figure 36, the concentration changes of VFAs, nitrate and ammonium,
sulphate, and the oxidation-reduction potential are shown.
From the ﬁgure, it can be seen that no VFAs remain in the system after
48 hours. At the beginning of the experiment, acetate and propionate are
present at equal concentrations. Moreover, the concentration of sulphate in-
creases from 59 g m−3 to 76 g m−3 (a 29% increase). The ammonium con-
centration decreases - from 66.1 gNH+
4 -N m−3 to 26.8 gNH+
4 -N m−3 (a 60%
decrease). The nitrate concentration increases from 0.44 gNO−
3 -N m−3 to
0.52 gNO−

Figure 36: Aerobic bioﬁlm biomarker transformation experiment. Measurements at
discrete time points throughout the experiment of (a) sulphate, (b) lactate, propi-
onate, formate, and acetate (c) nitrate and ammonium, and (d) continuous on-line

The increase in sulphate shows that reduced sulphur species were initially
present in the wastewater used. A much lower increase in sulphate was also
observed for the aerobic suspended biomass experiment. This implies that
either the initial concentration of sulphide differs, that sulphide oxidising
bacteria (SOB) are present in the bioﬁlm, or both.

Figure 37: Aerobic bioﬁlm biomarker transformation experiment. Measurements at
discrete time points throughout the experiment of temperature (squares), pH (cir-
cles), and dissolved oxygen (DO) (triangles).
Jiang et al. (2009) showed that SRB were found in the deeper layers of an
aerobic bioﬁlm (a depth deeper than 0.4 mm). Moreover, their results re-
vealed that the sulphate reduction rate was limited due to the absence of
substrate in the deeper layers (Jiang et al., 2009). They also assumed sul-
phide oxidation in the aerobic layers of the bioﬁlm in a manner similar to
the one proposed in Hvitved-Jacobsen et al. (2013). However, presuming
that the sulphate reduction is equal to the sulphide oxidation, the sulphate
concentration in the bulk should be somewhat constant. Hence, the increase
in sulphate can only come from a considerable presence of reduced sulphur
species.
The decrease of ammonium may, as previously discussed, be due to growth
of biomass. Moreover, the observed increase in nitrate may be due to nitriﬁ-
cation by autotrophic biomass in the bioﬁlm. Nonetheless, the observed de-
crease is higher than what was observed for the aerobic suspended biomass
experiment, which shows the additional impact of the bioﬁlm. The pH was
lower than observed for the anaerobic bioﬁlm experiment, however, the re-
moval of ammonium was still higher in the aerobic bioﬁlm experiment. This
could further indicate the presence of autotrophic bacteria in the aerobic
bioﬁlm. The sparging rate in the aerobic experiment was higher than the
sparging rate in the anaerobic bioﬁlm experiment, hence, the difference can
also simply be due to stripping (the average pH was 8.82).
The ORP and DO decreases when the obtained sample is measured which
is indicative of the bioﬁlm activity (cf. Figure 37). The circulation through
the ARBR also results in a temperature increase.

and the oxidation-reduction potential as functions of time are shown.
Over the course of the experiment, the concentration of sulphate, ammo-
nium, nitrate, and VFAs decrease. The initial concentration of sulphate is

Figure 38: Anaerobic bioﬁlm biomarker transformation experiment. Measurements
at discrete time points throughout the experiment of (a) sulphate, (b) Lactate, propi-
onate, formate, and acetate (c) nitrate and ammonium, and (d) continuous on-line
measurements of the oxidation-reduction potential (ORP). The small jumps in ORP
measurements are the ORP of the samples taken (the probe is physically removed
from the jacketed reactor into container of the sample being taken.
determined to be 60 g m−3. After three hours, the concentration decreases
to 43 g m−3, after 12 hours it has further decreased to 34 g m−3. After 24
hours the concentration of sulphate has decreased 65% to 21 g m−3, a con-
centration which is also determined after 48 hours. As for the suspended
biomass experiment, these ﬁndings indicate the presence of SRB. The sul-
phate concentration at t = 0.75 is 65 g m−3 which could indicate the pres-
ence of SOB in the bioﬁlm or chemical sulphide oxidation, however it may
also be ascribed to measurement uncertainty. The sulphate reduction seems
to follow an exponential decrease. In Hvitved-Jacobsen et al. (2013) the sul-
phate reduction is proposed to be modelled as sulphide production using
half-order kinetics. Sharma et al. (2008) suggest two different processes for
sulphide generation: one using fermentable substrate and the other using
VFAs. Both reaction rates are Monod-like. Jiang et al. (2009) suggests that
the SRB growth is modelled using a Monod-like rate where only fermenta-
tion products (VFAs) are utilised as electron donor.
The VFA content is found to be 84.2 gCOD m−3 at the start of the experiment.
Acetate is the dominating VFA with a concentration of 46.5 gCOD m−3,
propionate is found to be present at 36.7 gCOD m−3, and the remaining
1 gCOD m−3 is ascribed to formate. Lactate was not found in any of the
samples. The concentration of propionate decreases steadily from the begin-
ning of the experiment and is completely depleted after six hours. Formate
is found to be depleted after three hours. The concentration of acetate is
==PAGE:112==
92
results and discussion
rather constant at 41-48 gCOD m−3 but after 24 hours it has decreased to
28.5 gCOD m−3, and at the conclusion of experiment it is complete depleted.
Interestingly, propionate is found at a concentration of 2.9 gCOD m−3 at
t = 48hr, indicating on-going fermentation.
The measured ORP indicates very reductive conditions. At an ORP between
-250 mV to -300 mV, methanogenesis is possible in areas where sulphate
is depleted (Hvitved-Jacobsen et al., 2013). Sun et al. (2014) showed that
both SRB and methanogenic archaea (MA) could coexist in an anaerobic
sewer bioﬁlm. SRB were also shown to inhabit the outer layers of the bioﬁlm,
whereas the MA were predominant in the inner layers (Guisasola et al., 2008;
Sun et al., 2014). The ORP increases to approximately -50 mV after 36 hours
indicating anoxic conditions. The fact that the sulphate concentration is the
same in the sample taken after 24 and 48 hours could be ascribed to the
high ORP.
The nitrate is observed to have an initial concentration of 0.42 gN m−3 and
a ﬁnal concentration of 0.38 gN m−3 (it can be considered constant). The
ammonium decreases 26% from 58.9 gN m−3 to 43.6 gN m−3. This is a sig-
niﬁcantly smaller decrease than what was observed for the aerobic bioﬁlm.
The average pH was 9.49, the lowest pH 8.39, and the highest 9.80. Hence,
the environment can be considered highly alkaline. At this pH, NH3 is the
dominating specie in the ammonium/ammonia system, and it is likely to be
removed by stripping. As the sparging rate in the anaerobic experiment was
lower than for the aerobic experiment, this may explain the observations.
Moreover, in the H2S/HS−system, HS−is the dominating specie. The pro-
duced sulphide is thus likely to remain in the system and not be stripped
off. This could entail precipitation of iron sulphides.

The DO, temperature, and pH are shown in Figure 39. It can be seen that
while the pH is similar in the feed reactor and the taken samples, the tem-
perature increases 1◦C-3◦C in the ARBR. The time of sampling can also
easily be seen by the increase in dissolved oxygen. The sampling time takes
roughly four to six minutes depending on the volume needed (ﬂow rate of
4 L hr−1). This is enough time for the atmospheric air to diffuse into the
volume as this is completely devoid of oxygen. The samples are frozen in
==PAGE:113==

liquid nitrogen immediately upon sampling, hence the effect of the sudden
oxygenation can be considered negligible.

Replicate 2
(b) 48 hours
Figure 40: OUR experiment performed on samples taken from the anaerobic ARBR
reactor at (a) t = 24hr, and (b) t = 48hr. The measurements are performed every
eighth minute. The points shown are the average values over 80 minutes.
As opposed to the OUR found for the aerobic ARBR, it appears that no dis-
cernible amount of easily degradable substrate (SS + XS1) is present in the
centrifuged wastewater after 48 hours in the anaerobic ARBR. The OUR for
replicate 2 for the 24 hour sample suggests the presence of heterotrophic
bacteria and substrate, however the increase can ﬁrst be seen after 24 hours.
Prior to the observed increase, a considerable negative OUR can be observed
for both replicates. This is evidence of a pressure increase (remember, the
OxiTops measure the pressure change in the headspace). It can be seen for
both replicates to occur after 24 hours. Incidentally, this is when the sam-
ples taken at the conclusion of the biomarker experiment are put in the
incubator, hence, the pressure increase is most likely due to an increase in
temperature (the temperature in the laboratory was between 23◦C-26◦C and
the incubator temperature was set to 20◦C).
The concomitant decrease of VFAs and sulphate is a clear indication of the
presence of SRB in the bioreactor. The sulphate concentration seems to level
off after 24 hours. Coupled with exhaustion of the VFAs and the apparent
exhaustion of readily biodegradable substrate determined in the OUR exper-
iments could simply imply the complete removal of substrate in the system.
The lack of substrate then entails the commencement of decay of biomass.
Jiang et al. (2009) found that the reduced electron donor availability in the
anaerobic layers of a bioﬁlm in an aerobic sewer effectively impeded the
sulphate reduction. In an anaerobic bioﬁlm, Sun et al. (2014) found sulphate
to be depleted, whereas the soluble biodegradable COD was found to pene-
trate the bioﬁlm fully.
The CODt and sCOD measurements seem to suggest otherwise (cf. Fig-
ure 41). The COD in the system is almost exclusively present as soluble
COD. The initial increase due to the addition of the biomarkers dissolved
==PAGE:114==
94
results and discussion
in methanol is apparent. Interestingly, the sCOD does not attain a concen-
tration similar or below the initial concentration before the conclusion of
the experiment. This could indicate that methanol is not readily evaporated
but rather consumed by the biomass. The review on sulphate conversions in
wastewater treatment by Hao et al. (2014) shows that experiments in which
methanol had been used as substrate yielded sulfate reduction rates of 0.4 -
20.4 SO2−
4
g L−1 d−1. Within the ﬁrst 24 hours of the experiment 40 g L−1
SO2−
4
is used. To assess why the sulphate reduction comes to a standstill,
more work is needed, and preferably the bioﬁlm should be analysed using
a microelectrode.
The ﬁnal sCOD is measured to be 180 gCOD m−3 for the anaerobic exper-
iment, however this is higher than the measured CODt of 129 gCOD m−3
which is infeasible. Nonetheless, the COD remaining in the anaerobic
system is considerably higher than what was found for the aerobic sys-
tem where an sCOD concentration of 58.8 gCOD m−3 was determined at
t = 48hr. A possible explanation could be that the biomass present in the
anaerobic bioﬁlm readily utilises the added methanol due to their ability to
use fermentation products. Hence, the substrate available in the feed water
is not hydrolysed nor taken up to the same extent as by the aerobic bioﬁlm.

fermentation and hydrolysis
The data obtained from the 24 hours
and 48 hours analysis is coupled with the VFA analysis to calculate the
concentration of fermentable substrate (SF). The result is depicted in Figure
42.
From the ﬁgure, it can be seen that the readily biodegradable substrate is
mainly found as VFAs. At the beginning of the anaerobic ARBR experiment,
the VFAs make up almost 80% of the SS. After 24 hours, the VFAs are still
the dominating fraction, which is in stern contrast to the observations made
for the anaerobic suspended biomass/solids experiment in which the fer-
mentable substrate was the dominating fraction. In the bioﬁlm experiment,
the anaerobic system can then be considered to be limited by hydrolysis as
observed by Rudelle et al. (2011).

4.1.4
Effect of methanol
In the experiments, the analytes (biomarkers) are dissolved in methanol
and added to the system. As previously discussed, the added concentra-
tion results in an increase of COD. The theoretical oxygen demand (thOD)
of methanol is 1.5 gCOD gMeOH−1. The ﬁnal methanol concentration in
the system is 0.024%. This corresponds to a thOD of 285 g m−3. Methanol
is easily used as a substrate by the biomass (Tchobanoglous et al., 2013).
Consequently, OUR experiments with added methanol were performed in
parallel with the fractionation to elucidate the effect of this induced system
disturbance.
Methanol is a volatile compound, and will distribute itself in the two phases.
This can have an effect on the measurements as the OxiTop-Cs measure the
change in pressure in the overhead space. The equilibrium concentration
of methanol in the headspace can be estimated based on the Henry’s law
constant (H), and the assumption of no mass-transfer limitation (Trapp and
Matthies, 1998; Hvitved-Jacobsen et al., 2013). The data used in the calcula-
tion can be found in Table 37 in Appendix C. An aqueous concentration of
0.024% methanol corresponds to 5.98 mM. At equilibrium this corresponds
to a partial pressure of 0.0299 mbar. This apparent pressure increase in the
headspace of the BOD bottle is registered as a BOD decrease of 0.0329 mg
(cf. Eq. (3.16), calculations can be seen in Appendix C). For an even bigger
sample volume, the pressure change due to methanol evaporation is even
smaller. Consequently, the evaporation of methanol in the bottles does not
effect the data capture in any signiﬁcant way.

obtained without the addition of methanol.From the ﬁgure, it can be seen
that the curves are comparable during the ﬁrst 24 hours (the duration of
the fractionation experiment). For one of the experiments (grey diamonds),
the OUR increases from 6 gO2m−3hr−1 at t = 40 hr to 70 gO2 m−3 hr−1

at t = 48 hr. In all three replicates the OUR increases considerably after 45

biofilm experiment
The bioﬁlm experiments were done with cen-
trifuged and subsequently ﬁltered raw wastewater. As an increase had been
observed in the OUR experiment with methanol in the raw wastewater
used for the suspended biomass biotransformation experiment within the
48 hours of the experiment, the experimental assessment of the effect of
methanol on the ﬁltrate was prolonged to 72 hours. The result can be seen
in Figure 44.
In the ﬁgure, two distinct observations can be made. For the ﬁrst 40 hours
of the OUR experiment, there is not apparent difference between the repli-
cates with and without methanol added. However, after 40 hours the OUR
increases for the samples containing methanol. The OUR increases from 2.5
gO2m−3hr−1 to 15-24 gO2 m−3 hr−1 at t = 56 −60, and then immediately
decreases to an OUR of 2.5 gO2 m−3 hr−1. The decrease observed at t = 49
is due to opening of the incubator and hence increase in experiment temper-
ature as previously discussed.
The analysis of the second peak reveals a readily substrate concentration
of 215-232 gCOD m−3. This corresponds to 75%-82% of the theoretical oxy-
gen demand of the added methanol. Hence, the methanol has an effect on
the OUR experiment, however, it is not readily observable within the time
frame of the ARBR experiments. Nevertheless, the availability of methanol
as an electron donor and carbon source is likely to inﬂuence the growth of
biomass and substrate utilisation, especially in the bioﬁlm for the sulphate-
reducing bacteria and methanogenic archaea (Hao et al., 2014).

Figure 44: Comparison of the OUR curves with (triangles) and without (circles) the
addition of methanol. The experiment was done on ﬁltered raw wastewater used
in the bioﬁlm biotransformation experiment. The dashed vertical line delineates 48
hours, i.e. the conclusion of the biotransformation experiment.
All the biotransformation experiments performed in the laboratory were
done without lids on the jacketed reactors. Moreover, the reactors were
placed in a fume hood. It is therefore relevant to estimate the possible volatil-
isation rate of methanol. As seen in the previous paragraphs, the effect of
methanol in the beginning of each OUR experiment seems negligible. If it
readily evaporates, the possible negative implications may be ameliorated
(it may be completely ignored). The dimensionless Henry’s law’s constant
(air-water partitioning coefﬁcient) indicates that methanol does not volatilise
readily (Malcolm Pirnie, Inc., 1999), moreover the slow rate of volatilisation
further entails that the gas ﬁlm can be considered to control the rate of
volatilisation (Nazaroff and Alvarez-Cohen, 2001; Hvitved-Jacobsen et al.,
2013). The presence of an aeration disk which sparges either pressurised
atmospheric air or nitrogen further increases the complexity as well as the
rate at which methanol is removed from the aqueous phase. Experiments
should therefore be conducted to assess the rate at which methanol evapo-
rates from the matrices used in the experiments. This experiment should be
rather trivial and entail monitoring of either sCOD or methanol over time in
wastewater where the biomass has been inactivated. Alternatively, an exper-
iment could be done using tap water and then applying a correction factor,
as it also is done for experiments used to estimate the oxygen transfer rate
(KLa) (Tchobanoglous et al., 2013).
Other transformation experiments also included the spiking of methanol
dissolved biomarkers, as presented in Table 10. Of these, only the experi-
ments done by Senta et al. (2014) lasted more than 48 hours (for 72 hours).
Moreover, the glass bottles used therein were capped with cotton plugs and
the bottles only shaken intermittently. The methanol is likely to have had
an effect on both the growth of biomass as well as the microbial community
==PAGE:118==
98
results and discussion
present in these experiments, as bacteria able to readily use methanol as a
substrate will be favoured by natural selection.

4.2.1
Solid-liquid partitioning coefﬁcients
The experimental procedure to estimate the solid-liquid partitioning coefﬁ-
cients has been explained in Section 3.1. The outcome of the SSE-N2 and
SSE-O2 experiments (cf. Table 17) are tabulated in Table 26. In the estima-
tions, the sorption to walls and appurtenances are assumed to be adequately
described by the blank experiments in which mineral water was used. Fur-
thermore, the equilibrium was assumed to be attained within four hours.
From the table it can be seen that the compounds with the highest logKow
are missing (cannabinoids). The analytical method applied was not able to
determine the concentration of the added internal standard, making it im-
plausible to determine the concentration of the compounds. The analytical
results suggest that all compounds except codeine and morphine partition
onto the solids to varying degrees.
Cocaine was found to sorb to the bioﬁlm, whereas benzoylecgonine was
found to sorb to a degree that made it distinguishable from the abiotic
processes and sorption to reactor walls in all four experiments. Ecgonine
methyl ester and cocaethylene were found to sorbto both suspended solids
and bioﬁlm.

The 0 indicates that the sorption onto the suspended solids were not higher than the sorption
onto the reactor walls, hence, it was not possible to separate the two processes.
Heroin and 6-acetylmorphine were found to sorb to suspended matter, and
norcodeine to both suspended matter and the bioﬁlm. Surprisingly, M3G
was determined to sorb the most despite the fact that it has the lowest
logKow (cf. Figure 9). Clearly, this is infeasible. It is more likely that the
presence of β-glucuronidase gives rise to this decrease of M3G. This could
either be due to the lingering effect of extracellular enzymes or it could in-
dicate that the method chosen to inactivate the biomass is not sufﬁcient and
the concentration or reaction time for the inactivation inhibitor should be
increased before addition of biomarkers (Pierre et al., 2014). Moreover, this
would also obscure the assessment of the sorption potential of morphine. To
properly assess the sorption potential of morphine, all possible sources to
morphine should be absent from the solution.
Ketamine was found to sorb to the bioﬁlm, whereas norketamine was
found to sorb to the suspended matter. MDMA was only found to sorb
to the scraped-off bioﬁlm, wheras MDA was found to sorb to the sus-
pended matter. Mephedrone was found to sorb to the bioﬁlm. Methadone
and EDDP were both found to sorb to both the suspended matter and the
bioﬁlm. Lastly, methamphetamine was found to sorb to the suspended mat-
==PAGE:120==
100
results and discussion
ter, whereas amphetamine was found to sorb to suspended matter and
to sorb strongly to the bioﬁlm. Subedi and Kannan (2014) also found am-
phetamine to sorb strongly to suspended matter.
Senta et al. (2013) conducted sorption experiments with raw wastewater
and found comparable solid-liquid coefﬁcients for cocaine, benzoyl ecgo-
nine, and MDMA. However, they obtained higher coefﬁcients for EDDP,
morphine, and codeine.
The coefﬁcients determined experimentally were used in the modelling of
the biotransformation of the compounds, however, they should be used
with care as they were heavily inﬂuenced by the experimental set-up (rapid
changes in pH). In the transformation models, only the aqueous concen-
tration is assumed to be transformed, hence, if the compound was found to
sorb, the aqueous fraction subject to transformation can be determined from
fw =
1
1 + XSSKd
(4.1)
4.2.1.1
Effect of pH
The pH during the sorption experiments increased approximately 0.5 pH
and 1.0 pH for the aerobic and anaerobic experiment, respectively. Gulde
et al. (2014) found that the solid-liquid coefﬁcient determined in their ex-
periment with micropollutants having cationic-neutral speciation was not
pH-dependent. To conﬁrm or repudiate their ﬁndings, more work is needed
to ascertain whether or not the sorption of illicit drugs to suspended matter
is pH-dependent.
In Figure 45 the pKa for all the investigated biomarkers are superimposed
onto the pH measured for the sorption experiments. From the ﬁgure it can
be seen that the majority of the compounds observe a change in their specia-
tion due to the pH change. After approximately 30 minutes, the pH reached
a steady state in the aerobic experiment, whereas equilibrium was attained
after two hours in the anaerobic experiment.
4.2.2
Methamphetamine
In Figure 46 the results from the analyses of methamphetamine and am-
phetamine in the suspended biomass experiments are depicted.
Over the course of the experiments, methamphetamine does not show any
distinct changes in concentration (cf. Figure 46a). The concentration mea-
sured for both redox conditions align somewhat with the measurements
taken from the blank experiment. Hence, based on the experimental evi-
dence, methamphetamine seems to be stable. The results moreover reveal
that the employed analytical method overestimates the methamphetamine
concentration, as many of the measurements are above the spiked concen-
tration.

obic and anaerobic conditions and in the presence of bioﬁlm. Bagnall et al.
(2013) showed for a river microcosm that both compounds were susceptible
to biological transformation, however, in this study only amphetamine was
found to be transformed.
The scattered nature of the data makes it rather troublesome to estimate the
transformation rates of the pseudo-ﬁrst order model, and as a consequence
this was not done.
4.2.3
Cocaine
In Figure 48, the measurements and the model ﬁttings of cocaine and its ma-
jor metabolites are shown. The mathematical model describing the abiotic
transformation of the compounds is able to adequately capture the trends
for all compounds, however, for benzoylecgonine, the anaerobic abiotic for-
mation is not well captured by the model. It is evident that cocaine, ecgonine
methyl ester, and cocaethylene all undergo chemical hydrolysis. Moreover, it
is likely that both cocaine and cocaethylene are transformed to benzoylecgo-
nine. Under both redox conditions, cocaine degrades rapidly and attains a ﬁ-
nal concentration of 1.6 µg L−1 and 0.9 µg L−1 for the aerobic and anaerobic
experiment, respectively. The hydrolysis rate for the two redox conditions

Figure 48: (a) Aerobic and (b) anaerobic biotransformation, and (c) aerobic abiotic
and (d) anaerobic abiotic transformation of cocaine (COC, red ⋄), benzoylecgonine
(BE, blue △), ecgonine methyl ester (EME, black ▽), and cocaethylene (CE, green •)
by suspended biomass. The × marks outliers removed in the model calibration.
are not identical, although they are comparable. The observed differences
may be explained by the cumulative uncertainty related to the experimental
method, sampling, extraction, and chemical analysis. The main differences
between the two experiments are pH and oxygen saturation, and this may
affect kabiotic. Except for cocaethylene, the estimated abiotic rates are higher
for the anaerobic experiment. The estimated transformation rates are tabu-
lated in Table 27.
The experiment with the suspended biomass show that the compounds are
also transformed with biomass present. The aerobic model is able to satisfac-
torily capture the observations (R2 > 0.70). However, the anaerobic model
is unable to describe the experimental data (R2 < 0.1). This would suggest
that the dominant processes under anaerobic conditions are abiotic. The as-
sumption that the kabiotic determined in the blank experiment can be directly
transferred to the biotransformation experiment may not be correct as the
hydrolysis rate may be lower at higher ionic strengths.
In Figure 49 all the experimental results from the suspended biomass ex-
periments are shown. From this ﬁgure, it can be seen that the measured
concentrations for the biotransformation experiments and the blank experi-
ments are very similar. As just shown, it complicates the separation of the
contribution from the biotic and abiotic processes.

experiments with biomass and ▽and △are the blank experiments.
In Figure 50, the experimental and modelling results from the ARBR exper-
iments are shown. The abiotic model for the ARBR is able to capture the
concentration changes (all R2 > 0.9) better than the abiotic model for the
suspended experiments. As for the suspended biomass experiment, all the
compounds undergo chemical hydrolysis. Moreover, the abiotic transforma-
tion rates are higher for the anaerobic experiment, as was also observed in
the suspended biomass experiment. However, to assess whether or not the
observed differences between the redox condtions are statistically signiﬁ-
cant, further work is needed.
The estimated transformation rates are tabulated in Table 27. The abiotic
rates are in general estimated to be higher in the ARBR system (only excep-
tion is CE under anaerobic conditions). It could be due to the higher temper-
atures observed in the ARBR reactors during the experiment, as these are
not cooled and are therefore susceptible to heat transfer from the surround-
ings. The average temperature in the ARBR experiments was approximately
2-3◦C higher than in the suspended biomass experiments (cf. Table 36 in
Appendix B.5).
In Figure 51 all the data from the ARBR experiments are depicted. From the
ﬁgures it is evident that in these experiments, the dominating processes are
also the abiotic processes. The experimental evidence strongly suggests that
the transformation rates determined in other experiments for cocaine, ecgo-
nine methyl ester, and cocaethylene are not solely due to biotic processes

The other compounds appear to be stable. However, as heroin is trans-
formed to 6-MAM and morphine accumulates in the system, 6-MAM is

Figure 51: Aerobic and anaerobic biotransformation, and aerobic and anaerobic abi-
otic transformation of (a) cocaine, (b) benzoylecgonine, (c) ecgonine methyl ester,
and (d) cocaethylene in the bioﬁlm experiment. The grey markers are for the aerobic

also likely to be subject to abiotic transformation. The fact that 6-MAM does
not accumulate corroborates with this presumption.
The presence of the suspended biomass entails the near-complete removal
of heroin (<0.1 µg L−1) under aerobic conditions. The experimental results
from the anaerobic experiment are too few to draw any conclusions (only for
two samples could the concentration be determined), however the two mea-
surements do suggest removal. 6-MAM undergoes aerobic and anaerobic
biotransformation. The data suggest that the anaerobic conditions augment
the transformation as a lower concentration was attained in this experiment.
M3G is also transformed under both redox conditions, which is expected
as faecal bacteria present in the sewer systems have been shown to pro-
duce β-glucuronidase enzymes (D’Ascenzo et al., 2003; Baker and Kasprzyk-
Hordern, 2011). The transformation of 6-MAM is reﬂected in the increasing
morphine concentration. However, the increase is not observed to be to the
same extent as would be expected based on the transformation of 6-MAM
and M3G to morphine. Hence, it is likely that morphine is also transformed
by the suspended biomass under both redox conditions. Under aerobic con-
ditions morphine accumulates and attains a ﬁnal concentration of 15.4 µg
L−1, whereas under anaerobic conditions the ﬁnal concentration was found
to be 11.4 µg L−1. The transformation of morphine appears to be augmented
by the anaerobic conditions. Both codeine and norcodeine appear to be sta-
ble as the initial concentrations are comparable to the ﬁnal concentrations.

In Figure 53, the results from the bioﬁlm experiments are shown. As in the
blank experiments for the suspended experiments, heroin is shown to un-

for 6-MAM with a concomitant increase in morphine. M3G, codeine, and
norcodeine all appear to be stable and not subject to abiotic transformation.
The effect of the bioﬁlm is apparent. Heroin is almost completely degraded
after 12 hours under both redox conditions. After 48 hours, 6-MAM is also
completely removed. Moreover, M3G is also completely removed after 24
hours. The transformation chain of 6-MAM and M3G to morphine is evi-
dent from the decrease of these coupled with the concomitant increase in
morphine. In the model description, the difference in molar weight is taken

4.2.5
Ketamine
In Figure 54, the results from the suspended biomass experiments are
shown. For both ketamine and norketamine, the blank experiments sug-
gest that the compounds are stable and not subject to chemical hydrolysis.
Moreover, for the aerobic and anaerobic transformation experiments, the
compounds are also shown to be stable. The ﬁnal concentration of ketamine
was determined to be 8.9 µg L−1 and 8.1 µg L−1 for the aerobic and anaer-
obic experiment, respectively. For norketamine, the ﬁnal concentration for
the anaerobic experiment is 7.2 µg L−1. However, these measurements are
all higher than the concentration measured at the beginning of the experi-
ment.
In Figure 55, the results from the ARBR experiments are shown. For both
compounds, the abiotic transformation appears to be more pronounced than
the biotic transformation. For ketamine, the ﬁnal measurements are either
above the spiked concentration or similar to this. A “jump” can be seen from
the penultimate measurement to the ﬁnal measurement. This suggests prob-

In conclusion, the experimental evidence suggests that ketamine and nor-

Kasprzyk-Hordern (2011) and Lin et al. (2014) also concluded that ketamine
and norketamine were stable in an aqueous environment.
4.2.6
MDMA
In Figure 56, the results from the suspended biomass experiments are
shown. In the blank experiments, both MDMA and MDA are stable and
do not transform. In the biotransformation experiment, MDMA appears to
undergo transformation under aerobic conditions. The initial concentration
in the aerobic experiment was determined to be 10.6 µg L−1, and the ﬁnal
concentration 9.1 µg L−1. For the anaerobic experiment, the ﬁnal concentra-
tion was higher (7.6 µg L−1) than the initial concentration measured (6.6 µg
L−1) suggesting analytical difﬁculties not corrected for by the addition of
the internal standard.
For MDA, the measurements are scattered (large standard deviations for
the duplicates), and the initial concentration in the aerobic experiment was
determined to be 12.6 µg L−1, which is higher than the spiked concentra-
tion and background concentration. For the anaerobic experiment it was
determined to be 9.4 µg L−1. The ﬁnal concentrations were determined to
be 9.6 µg L−1 and 7.9 µg L−1 for the aerobic and anaerobic experiment,
respectively. Hence, MDA appears to be biotransformed under anaerobic
conditions.

Figure 57: Aerobic and anaerobic biotransformation, and aerobic and anaerobic abi-

In Figure 57, the results from the ARBR experiments are shown. From the
blank experiments it can be seen that MDMA is stable under both redox con-
ditions as observed in the other blank experiment. MDA, however, seems to
be transformed abiotically under anaerobic conditions. The initial concen-
tration was determined to be 10.9 µg L−1 and the ﬁnal concentration was
found to be 6.8 µg L−1. However, the penultimate measurement yielded a
concentration of 9.8 µg L−1, hence, the observed decrease may be due to
analytical difﬁculties. It would also be contradictory to the data from the
blank experiment for the suspended biomass experiment.
In the anaerobic ARBR experiment, MDMA appears to be subject to biotrans-
formation. The initial concentration was determined to be 10.9 µg L−1and
the ﬁnal was determined to be 8.5 µg L−1. A model was ﬁtted to the ex-
perimental data, however, it was not possible to obtain a good ﬁt between
the model predictions and the experimental data, indicating the limitations
of a pseudo-ﬁrst order model to describe the anaerobic transformation of
MDMA. In the aerobic ARBR experiment the data indicate that MDMA is
stable. MDA appears to be stable under both redox conditions in the ARBR
experiments.
==PAGE:132==
112
results and discussion
As previously mentioned, Thai et al. (2014) found MDMA to be stable in
a sewer experiment under different redox conditions, however, Kasprzyk-
Hordern and Baker (2012) found evidence of biotransformation of MDMA.
The data presented in this thesis suggests that MDMA can be transformed
by the sewer bioﬁlm under anaerobic conditions.
4.2.7
Mephedrone
In Figure 58, the results from the suspended biomass experiments are
shown. The blank experiments show that mephedrone is transformed abiot-
ically. The ﬁnal concentrations are 8.8 µg L−1 and 7.05 µg L−1 for the aero-
bic and anaerobic blank experiment, respectively. The data also reveals that
mephedrone is transformed biotically. The ﬁnal concentrations were 2.3 µg
L−1 and 4.7 µg L−1 for the aerobic and anaerobic biotransformation exper-
iments, respectively. Interestingly, the experimental evidence suggests that
mephedrone is more readily transformed under aerobic redox conditions.

Figure 58: Aerobic and anaerobic biotransformation, and aerobic and anaerobic abi-
otic transformation of mephedrone in the (a) suspended biomass/solids experiment,
and (b) bioﬁlm experiment. The grey markers are for the aerobic experiments, the

The abiotic and biotic transformation rates were quantiﬁed by ﬁtting the
pseudo-ﬁrst order mathematical model to the observations. The resulting
ﬁts are shown in Figure 59 and the obtained transformation rates are tab-
ulated in Table 28. A better ﬁt can be seen for the aerobic experiments
(R2 > 0.93). The obtained parameters indicate that the anaerobic abiotic
transformation rate is twice as large as the aerobic counterpart. This may be
ascribed to the difference in pH and resulting speciation of the compound.
However, further work is needed to test this hypothesis.
When the suspended biomass is present, the biotic transformation rate is
observed to be almost 10-fold higher in the aerobic experiment compared
to the anaerobic experiment. The aerobic heterotrophic activity greatly aug-
ments the removal of mephedrone. The presence of biomass in the anaerobic
experiment also augments the removal rate, however not to the same extent.
This corroborates well with the fact that a higher microbial activity was
observed in the aerobic system compared to the anaerobic system (when
comparing the COD decrease over time).

In Figure 58, the results from the ARBR experiments are shown. The blank
experiments also suggests that mephedrone is transformed abiotically under
both redox conditions. The ﬁnal concentrations were 7.5 µg L−1 and 7.3 µg
L−1 for the aerobic and anaerobic blank experiment, respectively.
The presence of the bioﬁlm augments the transformation of mephedrone.
The ﬁnal concentrations measured were 1.7 µg L−1 and 3.7 µg L−1 for
the aerobic and anaerobic experiment, respectively. The ﬁndings from
the bioﬁlm experiment corroborate with the ﬁndings from the suspended
biomass experiment, i.e. mephedrone appears to be more readily trans-
formed under aerobic redox conditions. The model was ﬁtted to the ob-
served data and the resulting curve ﬁttings are shown in Figure 60 and the
obtained transformation rates are tabulated in Table 28.

0.75), The estimated abiotic transformation rates are similar for both exper-
iments. The biotic transformation rate for the aerobic experiment was esti-
mated to be 50-fold higher than for the anaerobic experiment. Despite the
fact that the anaerobic bioﬁlm was determined to be thicker than the aerobic
bioﬁlm, the aerobic bioﬁlm is able to more readily transform mephedrone.
Again, the activity in the anaerobic reactor was found to be lower than in
the aerobic reactor when the transformation of carbon is used as the metric.
Moreover, for the aerobic bioﬁlm, mephedrone was determined to sorp to a
large extent (Kd = 1.61 L g−1). This has implications for the estimation of

4.2.8
Methadone
In Figure 61, the results from the suspended biomass experiments are
shown. From the ﬁgure, it is evident that methadone undergoes abiotic
and biotic transformation under both redox conditions. The same is valid
for EDDP except that no abiotic transformation is observed under aerobic
conditions.

Figure 61: Aerobic and anaerobic biotransformation, and aerobic and anaerobic abi-
otic transformation of (a) methadone, and (b) EDDP in the suspended biomass ex-
periment. The grey markers are for the aerobic experiments, the black are for the

Consequently, the proposed mathematical models describing the pseudo-
ﬁrst order transformation were applied to the experimental data to deter-
mine the abiotic transformation rate and the biotransformation rate. The re-
sults of the model ﬁttings are shown in Figure 61. The abiotic transformation
is adequately described for the anaerobic experiment for both compounds
(R2 > 0.95), however, for the aerobic abiotic transformation the model ﬁt
for EDDP is poor (R2 = 0.31), although the transformation of methadone is
adequately captured by the model (R2 > 0.90).
Under aerobic conditions, the presence of suspended biomass result in the
rapid degradation of methadone. After three hours, the concentration is
below 1 µg L−1 and after 48 hours the concentration has further decreased
to 0.29 µg L−1. After 48 hours the concentration of EDDP has decreased to
6.4 µg L−1. The model is not able to capture these transformation rates. The
rapid initial removal of methadone does not result in a concomitant increase
in EDDP.
Under anaerobic conditions the compounds are also transformed, however,
not with same rate as in the aerobic experiment. The ﬁnal concentration
of methadone and EDDP are 3.2 and 6.4 µg L−1, respectively. Importantly,
the ﬁnal concentration of methadone is likely heavily inﬂuenced by the fact
that the anaerobic suspended system was aerobic 41-47 hours after the spik-
ing. Consequently, the determined concentration at 48 hours is not repre-
sentative of anaerobic biotransformation, and it was therefore removed in
the model ﬁtting and parameter estimation. The model ﬁt is however not
satisfactory (R2 < 0.2). It indicates that the model description is not able to
encapsulate the occurring processes and as such needs further development.
Furthermore, as mentioned previously, the assumption that the abiotic rate
==PAGE:136==

determined in the blank experiments is valid for the biotransformation ex-
periment may not be appropriate.

In Figure 63, the results from the ARBR experiments are shown. As for
the experiment with the suspended biomass, both compounds are readily
transformed under both redox conditions. In this experiment, EDDP is also
shown to transform abiotically under anaerobic conditions. Consequently,
the abiotic transformation rates and the biotransformation rates were esti-
mated by calibration of the model to the observed data.
The abiotic transformation of both compounds seem to be more pronounced
in the ARBR experiments. The model is able to capture the abiotic degrada-
tion rates satisfactorily (R2 > 0.85). For both compounds, the ﬁnal concen-
tration attained is lower in the anaerobic experiment. For methadone the
difference is approximately 50%, and for methadone it is almost 33%. The
key difference between the two experiments is the pH. At higher pH, a
larger fraction of both compounds are neutral.
The presence of the bioﬁlm augments the transformation of the compounds.
Under aerobic conditions, methadone is readily degraded, and 12 hours
after the spiking, the concentration is below 1 µg L−1. The anaerobic trans-
formation is not as rapid, however, as opposed to the experiment with sus-
pended biomass, the bioﬁlm is able to transform methadone. The model

methadone and EDDP are prone to transformation in the sewer, hence, their
use as biomarkers is dubious even in sewers with small hydraulic reten-
tion times. The rapid transformation of methadone is in stark contrast to
other experiments which found methadone to be rather stable (Castiglioni
et al., 2006; Senta et al., 2014). Moreover, the experimental evidence sug-
gests that the bioﬁlm transforms the compounds at a rate higher than the
suspended biomass under anaerobic conditions. The reason for this may be
the possible higher microbial diversity present in the stratiﬁed layers of the
bioﬁlm. Under aerobic conditions the suspended biomass seems to trans-
form methadone faster than the bioﬁlm although the model ﬁtting suggests
otherwise.
4.2.9
THC
It was not possible to recover or detect the added deuterated internal stan-
dards for THC, THC-OH, THC-COOH, CBN, or CBN in most of the samples
taken from the experiments. As a result, it has not been possible to present
any experimental data for these compounds.
4.2.10
Effect of pH
Differences in the abiotic transformation were observed between the aer-
obic and anaerobic (blank) experiments. It is likely to be correlated with
the pH. However, more work is needed to further elucidate whether or not
the pH affects the transformation rate in the sewer system. As previously
mentioned, Gulde et al. (2014) have found the biotransformation rate to be
pH-dependent in activated sludge.
==PAGE:139==
4.3 further work
119
4.3
further work
The thesis was carried out not only to investigate and assess the posed hy-
potheses and objectives but also to identify areas in need of more research
and work. Hence, recommendations for future work will be presented in
the following.
The efﬁciency of the annular rotating bioﬁlm reactors with regard to util-
isation, uptake and transformation of macro-nutrients (carbon, nitrogen,
phosphorous, and sulphur species) should be assessed. Furthermore, the
methane and hydrogen sulphide production should also be quantiﬁed. This
would help to close the sulphur mass balance. The hydraulics and mixing
of the annular rotating bioﬁlm reactors should be assessed by a carefully de-
signed tracer study, preferably employing a non-toxic and inert tracer – i.e.
NaCl at rather low concentrations dissolved in tap water. Moreover, an ex-
periment should be conducted to properly assess the effect of methanol on
the biomass growth and substrate utilisation (both for suspended biomass
and bioﬁlm). Instead of measuring COD, the concentration of methanol
should be explicitly quantiﬁed. Moreover, a proper characterisation of the
microbiological community present in the bioﬁlms using molecular tech-
niques should be performed to assess the presence of sulphate-reducing
and sulphate-oxidising bacteria, autotrophic bacteria, and methanogenic ar-
chaea. Microelectrode measurements to characterise the penetration depth
of macro-nutrients and the vertical structure of the bioﬁlm, as described in
Jiang et al. (2009) and Sun et al. (2014), could also be performed. This is not
only to understand the transformation of xenobiotics but also to contribute
to the knowledge base on bioﬁlms formed under sewer-like conditions. The
large surface area to volume ratio makes it difﬁcult to transfer the ﬁndings
from the thesis to a “real” sewerage system (Thai et al., 2014).
With regard to the biotransformation of biomarkers, experiments could be
conducted with fewer compounds spiked into the system. Deuterated par-
ent compounds could be spiked and then the deuterated metabolites should
be chosen as the targeted analytes. Using this approach, the unique transfor-
mation products can be determined. To assess the deconjugation rate of con-
jugated species these could also be spiked in a separate experiment.he effect
of pH on the abiotic and biotic transoformation rate should also be assessed
(Gulde et al., 2014). Helbling et al. (2012) found that the COD removal did
not correlate with the biotransformation of xenobiotics in activated sludge.
It is therefore interesting to identify which processes in the sewer correlate
with the biotransformation.
The experimental method adapted to obtain the parameters needed for the
WATS model needs to be validated. The conducted experiments should be
repeated without the addition of biomarkers to obtain parameters and data
to be used to construct models describing the aerobic and anaerobic primary
metabolism in the systems. Finally, the coupling of a model describing the
primary metabolism and the co-metabolism of the xenobiotics should also
be formulated and implemented. This was sought to be done in this thesis,
however due to experimental and temporal complications, this was not pos-
sible. In general, more work is also needed in order to be able to properly
==PAGE:140==
120
results and discussion
describe the observations using models for micropollutant removal (Pomiès
et al., 2013; Liu et al., 2015).
==PAGE:141==
5
C O N C L U S I O N
The aim of this thesis was to assess the transformation of drug biomarkers
by the suspended sewer biomass, and a sewer bioﬁlm while also monitor-
ing the primary metabolism of the biomass. Furthermore, the sorption of
the drug biomarkers to the suspended solids, and the bioﬁlm was also as-
sessed. Lastly, the abiotic transformation was also assessed by the use of
blank experiments with mineral water. All experiments were conducted un-
der aerobic, and anaerobic redox conditions.
The WATS model was used to describe the primary metabolism of the aer-
obic suspended heterotrophic biomass and calibrated to experimental data.
The activity of the suspended biomass was estimated to result in an ﬁve-fold
increase of the suspended aerobic heterotrophic biomass within 12 hours of
the aerobic experiment. The easily biodegradable substrate was estimated to
be depleted within 16 hours of the experiment resulting in the endogenous
decay of the biomass.
The activity of the anaerobic suspended biomass resulted in a 20% decrease
of the sulphate concentration and the production of acetate. This is indica-
tive of sulphate-reducing bacteria.
For the aerobic annular rotating bioﬁlm reactor experiments, sulphate was
found to be produced as the concentration increased 40% over 48 hours. This
is suggestive of anaerobic conditions present in the wastewater used for the
experiment. Moreover, it also indicates the presence of sulphide-oxidising
bacteria in the aerobic bioﬁlm. Moreover, a slight increase in the nitrate con-
centration could signify the presence of autotrophic bacteria in the bioﬁlm.
In the anaerobic annular rotating bioﬁlm reactor, the sulphate concentration
was reduced more than 70%. Moreover, the data suggested that all readily
biodegradable substrate was consumed by the biomass and that the lack
of substrate (electron donor) effectively hampered the further reduction of
sulphate. The VFA analyses suggested on-going fermentation throughout
the experiment. The measured oxidation-reduction potential revealed that
methanogensis would be possible in the bioﬁlm layers if sulphate was de-
pleted.
The biomarkers were dissolved in methanol. Experiments conducted to as-
sess the effect on the biomass growth suggested that methanol was not
utilised by the suspended aerobic biomass. However, for the anaerobic an-
nular rotating bioﬁlm experiments, it was found likely that the biomass was
able to utilise the exogenous carbon.
From the transformation experiments conducted, the following can be con-
cluded regarding the fate of the biomarkers:
121
==PAGE:142==
122
conclusion
With regard to sorption, all compounds except codeine and morphine were
found to sorb to either the suspended solids/biomass or the bioﬁlm to an
extent that made it discernible from the abiotic processes and possible sorp-
tion to the reactor walls. Consequently, the sorbed fraction should not be
ignored when calculating the mass present in a sewer sample.
Methamphetamine was found to be stable under both redox conditions
and when exposed to suspended and attached biomass. Amphetamine was
found to be transformed by the aerobic bioﬁlm. Cocaine, benzoylecgonine,
ecgonine methyl ester, and cocaethylene were all found to be abiotically
transformed under both redox conditions. This made it difﬁcult to ascertain
whether or not the presence of biomass augmented the transformation.
Heroin was found to be abiotically transformed under both redox condi-
tions. The transformation of heroin was augmented by the presence of both
suspended biomass and the bioﬁlm. 6-acetylmorphine and morphine-3-β-
D-glucuronide were found to be transformed to morphine by the attached
and suspended biomass under both redox conditions. Under anaerobic con-
ditions, the transformation rate appeared to be higher. Morphine was found
to be transformed by the suspended biomass under both redox conditions,
and the aerobic bioﬁlm was found to augment the transformation of mor-
phine. Codeine appeared to be stable under both redox conditions and with
and without the presence of biomass. Norcodeine was found to be trans-
formed by the anaerobic bioﬁlm.
Ketamine and norketamine were found to be stable under all test conditions.
The anaerobic bioﬁlm was shown to transform MDMA, which in the other
experiments was found to be stable. MDA was found to be stable under all
test conditions.
Mephedrone was shown to undergo both abiotic and biotic transformation
under different redox conditions, and it was possible to describe this using
the ASM-X model. The presence of sewer biomass (both suspended biomass
and bioﬁlm) augmented the transformation rate. The aerobic transformation
rate was found to be 10 and 50 times higher than the anaerobic transforma-
tion rate for the suspended biomass and the bioﬁlm, respectively.
Methadone and EDDP were both transformed abiotically and biotically un-
der aerobic and anaerobic conditions. Under aerobic conditions, the rate
at which the compounds were transformed was considerably higher than
under anaerobic conditions. The presence of the bioﬁlm considerably aug-
mented the transformation of both compounds. The model was able to de-
scribe the abiotic transformation of methadone and EDDP. It was however
not able to adequately describe the aerobic biotransformation of the com-
pounds. Moreover, the experiments suggests that methadone is transformed
more to another metabolite than EDDP.

b.2

the bioﬁlm, the surface area, and the TSS content of the coupons. The data
for the ARBR are listed in Table 35. The gap is the distance between the
inner cylinder and the outer cylinder (i.e. the reactor void).

Firstly, the surface area of the reactor was determined. The inner surface of
the reactor consists of the two cylinder surfaces, and the bottom and the top
of the gap (with an area corresponding to the outer cylinder area minus the
inner cylinder area). Hence, the inner surface can be determined thus
A = 2 πrinner h + 2 π router h + 2 (π (router)2) −(π (rinner)2)
A = 2π 0.045 m 0.25 m + 2π 0.057 m 0.25 m + 2 (π (0.057 m)2) −π (0.045 m)2)
A = 0.0706 m2 + 0.0895 m2 + 0.00769m2 = 0.168 m2
Two coupons from each reactor were scraped off into containers containing
two liters of water. The resulting TSS content was for the anaerobic ARBR

approximately 297 mg per coupon in the anaerobic ARBR, and 141 mg per
coupon in the aerobic ARBR (cf. Table 34). The surface area of a coupon is
0.0035 m2. Assuming a homogeneous bioﬁlm, this results in a bioﬁlm mass
in each reactor of

Before the coupons were removed, the ARBRs were completely ﬁlled, and
the volume contained in the reactors was measured. The anaerobic reactor
could contain 0.790 L, and the aerobic reactor could contain 0.836 L. Hence,
the volume of the bioﬁlm in the reactors is
Vbioﬁlm = Vempty −Vmeasured
Vaerobic, bioﬁlm = 0.961 L −0.836 L = 0.125 L
Vanaerobic, bioﬁlm = 0.961 L −0.790 L = 0.171 L

However, though these values can be used to describe the COD of the
bioﬁlms, it does not mean that it purely can be ascribed to active biomass,
as inert particulates are predominant in a mature bioﬁlm (Jiang et al., 2009).
b.3
determination of function describing the change in hy-
draulic retention time
As discussed in the main part of the thesis, samples are drawn at discrete
time points reducing the volume in the system and hence changing the hy-
draulic retention time in the feed reactor. This has implications for the rela-
tive time spent in the ARBR – i.e. when the volume in the system decreases,
the relative time spent in the ARBR increases. Consequently, the volume
drawn at each time point was meticulously measured and noted. Moreover,
content was also lost due to spillage and evaporation. This was quantiﬁed
by measuring the ﬁnal volume and subtracting this from the expected vol-
ume assuming no loss. The loss was equally (and linearly) distributed over
the duration of the experiment (i.e. f (t) =
volume lost
duration of experimentt). The
result showing the volume in the system as a function of time is shown in
Figure 68.
The function describing the decrease in volume can be though to be formu-
lated as
